	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Assyria</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about ancient Assyria. For the geographic and cultural location, see <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_homeland" title="Assyrian homeland">Assyrian homeland</a>. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Assyria_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Assyria (disambiguation)">Assyria (disambiguation)</a></div>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Major Mesopotamian East Semitic kingdom</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Assyrian Empire" redirects here. For the most powerful stage of the ancient Assyrian state, see <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">Neo-Assyrian Empire</a>.</div>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:258px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NC_Mesopotamia_sites.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b8/NC_Mesopotamia_sites.jpg/256px-NC_Mesopotamia_sites.jpg" decoding="async" width="256" height="209" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b8/NC_Mesopotamia_sites.jpg/384px-NC_Mesopotamia_sites.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b8/NC_Mesopotamia_sites.jpg/512px-NC_Mesopotamia_sites.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1320" data-file-height="1080" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NC_Mesopotamia_sites.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map showing the approximate location of the geographical region referred to as "Assyria".</div></div></div>
<p><b>Assyria</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ə/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span><span title="/ɪər/: &#39;ear&#39; in &#39;near&#39;">ɪər</span><span title="/i/: &#39;y&#39; in &#39;happy&#39;">i</span><span title="/ə/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">ə</span></span>/</a></span></span>), also called the <b>Assyrian Empire</b>, was a <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamian</a> kingdom and <a href="/wiki/Empire" title="Empire">empire</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Near_East" title="Ancient Near East">ancient Near East</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a> that existed as a state from perhaps as early as the 25th century BC (in the form of the <a href="/wiki/Assur" title="Assur">Assur</a> city-state<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>) until its collapse between 612 BC and 609 BC - spanning the periods of the <a href="/wiki/Early_Bronze_Age" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Bronze Age">Early</a> to <a href="/wiki/Middle_Bronze_Age" class="mw-redirect" title="Middle Bronze Age">Middle Bronze Age</a> through to the late <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-edwards_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-edwards-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> From the end of the seventh century BC (when the <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian" class="mw-redirect" title="Neo-Assyrian">Neo-Assyrian</a> state fell) to the mid-seventh century AD, it survived as a <a href="/wiki/Geopolitical" class="mw-redirect" title="Geopolitical">geopolitical</a> entity,<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> for the most part ruled by foreign powers such as the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthian</a><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> and early <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sasanian Empires</a><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> between the mid-second century BC and late third century AD, the final part of which period saw <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> become a major centre of <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Christianity" title="Syriac Christianity">Syriac Christianity</a> and the birthplace of the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_East" title="Church of the East">Church of the East</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Winkler_p._1_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Winkler_p._1-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A largely <a href="/wiki/Semitic_languages" title="Semitic languages">Semitic</a>-speaking realm, Assyria was centred on the <a href="/wiki/Tigris" title="Tigris">Tigris</a> in <a href="/wiki/Upper_Mesopotamia" title="Upper Mesopotamia">Upper Mesopotamia</a> (modern northern <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>, northeastern <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>, southeastern <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> and the northwestern fringes of <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>). The Assyrians came to rule powerful empires in several periods. Making up a substantial part of the greater Mesopotamian "<a href="/wiki/Cradle_of_civilization" title="Cradle of civilization">cradle of civilization</a>", which included <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumer</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_Empire" title="Akkadian Empire">Akkadian Empire</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonia</a>, Assyria reached the height of technological, scientific and cultural achievements for its time. At its peak, the <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">Neo-Assyrian Empire</a> of 911 to 609 BC stretched from <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a> and the <a href="/wiki/East_Mediterranean" class="mw-redirect" title="East Mediterranean">East Mediterranean</a> to <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, and from present-day <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> and eastern <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Albert_Kirk_Grayson_1972_108_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Albert_Kirk_Grayson_1972_108-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The name "Assyria" originates with the Assyrian state's original capital, the ancient city of <a href="/wiki/Assur" title="Assur">Aššur</a>, which dates to c. 2600 BC – originally one of a number of <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">Akkadian</a>-speaking city-states in Mesopotamia. In the 25th and 24th centuries BC, Assyrian kings were <a href="/wiki/Pastoral_society" title="Pastoral society">pastoral</a> <a href="/wiki/Leader" class="mw-redirect" title="Leader">leaders</a>. From the late 24th century BC, the Assyrians became subject to <a href="/wiki/Sargon_of_Akkad" title="Sargon of Akkad">Sargon of Akkad</a>, who united all the Akkadian- and <a href="/wiki/Sumerian_language" title="Sumerian language">Sumerian</a>-speaking peoples of Mesopotamia under the <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_Empire" title="Akkadian Empire">Akkadian Empire</a>, which lasted from <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 2334 BC to 2154 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> After the Assyrian Empire fell from power, the greater remaining part of Assyria formed a geopolitical region and <a href="/wiki/Province" title="Province">province</a> of other empires, although between the mid-2nd century BC and late 3rd century AD a patchwork of small independent Assyrian kingdoms arose in the form of <a href="/wiki/Assur" title="Assur">Assur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adiabene" title="Adiabene">Adiabene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Osroene" title="Osroene">Osroene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beth_Nuhadra" class="mw-redirect" title="Beth Nuhadra">Beth Nuhadra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beth_Garmai" title="Beth Garmai">Beth Garmai</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hatra" title="Hatra">Hatra</a>.
</p><p>The region of Assyria fell under the successive control of the <a href="/wiki/Median_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Median Empire">Median Empire</a> of 678 to 549 BC, the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid Empire</a> of 550 to 330 BC, the <a href="/wiki/Macedonian_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Macedonian Empire">Macedonian Empire</a> (late 4th century BC), the <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid Empire</a> of 312 to 63 BC, the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthian Empire</a> of 247 BC to 224 AD, the <a href="/wiki/Assyria_(Roman_province)" title="Assyria (Roman province)">Roman Empire</a> (from 116 to 118 AD) and the <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sasanian Empire</a> of 224 to 651 AD. The <a href="/wiki/Arab" class="mw-redirect" title="Arab">Arab</a> <a href="/wiki/Early_Muslim_conquests" title="Early Muslim conquests">Islamic conquest</a> of the area in the mid-seventh century finally dissolved Assyria (<a href="/wiki/Assuristan" class="mw-redirect" title="Assuristan">Assuristan</a>) as a single entity, after which the remnants of the <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">Assyrian people</a> (by now <a href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">Christians</a>) gradually became an ethnic, linguistic, cultural and religious minority in the <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_homeland" title="Assyrian homeland">Assyrian homeland</a>, surviving there to this day as an <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples" title="Indigenous peoples">indigenous</a> people of the region.<sup id="cite_ref-Parpola,_Simo_2004_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Parpola,_Simo_2004-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Assyria was also sometimes known as <a href="/wiki/Subartu" title="Subartu">Subartu</a> and <i>Azuhinum</i> prior to the rise of the <a href="/wiki/City-state" title="City-state">city-state</a> of Ashur, after which it was <a href="/wiki/A%C5%A1%C5%A1%C5%ABr%C4%81yu" class="mw-redirect" title="Aššūrāyu">Aššūrāyu</a>, and after its fall, from 605 BC through to the late seventh century AD variously as <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Assyria" title="Achaemenid Assyria">Achaemenid Assyria</a>, and also referenced as Atouria, <a href="/wiki/Ator" title="Ator">Ator</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hathor" title="Hathor">Athor</a>, and sometimes as <i>Syria</i> which etymologically derives from Assyria<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> according to <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a>, <i>Syria</i> (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>), <i>Assyria</i> (<a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>) and <a href="/wiki/As%C5%8Drist%C4%81n" class="mw-redirect" title="Asōristān">Asōristān</a> (Middle Persian). "Assyria" can also refer to the geographic region or heartland where Assyria, its empires and the <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">Assyrian people</a> were (and still are) centered.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples" title="Indigenous peoples">indigenous</a> modern <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Aramaic_languages" title="Eastern Aramaic languages">Eastern Aramaic-speaking</a> <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">Assyrian</a> Christian ethnic minority in northern <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>, north east <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>, southeast <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> and northwest <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> are the descendants of the ancient Assyrians (see <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_continuity" title="Assyrian continuity">Assyrian continuity</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-H.W.F._Saggs_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H.W.F._Saggs-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> As Babylonia is called after the city of Babylon, Assyria means "land of <a href="/wiki/Assur" title="Assur">Asshur</a>"<sup id="cite_ref-Ragozin_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ragozin-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Etymologically, <i>Assyria</i> is connected to the <a href="/wiki/Name_of_Syria" title="Name of Syria">name of <i>Syria</i></a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Renan_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Renan-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> with both being ultimately derived from the <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">Akkadian</a> <i lang="akk-Latn" title="Akkadian-language romanization"><a href="/wiki/Assur" title="Assur">Aššur</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Tvedtnes_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tvedtnes-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Theodor_N%C3%B6ldeke" title="Theodor Nöldeke">Theodor Nöldeke</a> in 1881 was the first to give philological support to the assumption that <i>Syria</i> and <i>Assyria</i> have the same etymology,<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> a suggestion going back to <a href="/wiki/John_Selden" title="John Selden">John Selden</a> (1617). A 21st-century discovery of the <a href="/wiki/%C3%87inek%C3%B6y_inscription" title="Çineköy inscription">Çineköy inscription</a> also confirmed that Syria, being a Greek corruption of the name Assyria, is ultimately derived from the Assyrian term <i>Aššūrāyu</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Frye_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Frye-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-history">Pre-history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Pre-history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Letter_Luenna_Louvre_AO4238.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Letter_Luenna_Louvre_AO4238.jpg/220px-Letter_Luenna_Louvre_AO4238.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Letter_Luenna_Louvre_AO4238.jpg/330px-Letter_Luenna_Louvre_AO4238.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Letter_Luenna_Louvre_AO4238.jpg/440px-Letter_Luenna_Louvre_AO4238.jpg 2x" data-file-width="755" data-file-height="582" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Letter_Luenna_Louvre_AO4238.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Official letter sent by the high-priest Lu'enna to the king of <a href="/wiki/Lagash" title="Lagash">Lagash</a> (maybe <a href="/wiki/Urukagina" title="Urukagina">Urukagina</a>), informing him of his son's death in combat, c. 2400 BC, found in <a href="/wiki/Girsu" title="Girsu">Girsu</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In prehistoric times, the region that was to become known as Assyria (and Subartu) was home to a <a href="/wiki/Neanderthal" title="Neanderthal">Neanderthal</a> culture such as has been found at the <a href="/wiki/Shanidar_Cave" title="Shanidar Cave">Shanidar Cave</a>. The earliest <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> sites in what will be Assyria were the <a href="/wiki/Jarmo" title="Jarmo">Jarmo</a> culture c. 7100 BC, the  <a href="/wiki/Halaf_culture" title="Halaf culture">Halaf culture</a> c. 6100 BC, and the <a href="/wiki/Hassuna_culture" title="Hassuna culture">Hassuna culture</a> c. 6000 BC.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">Akkadian-speaking</a> people (the earliest historically-attested <a href="/wiki/Semitic-speaking_peoples" class="mw-redirect" title="Semitic-speaking peoples">Semitic-speaking people</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-Christopher_Woods_2008,_p.83_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Christopher_Woods_2008,_p.83-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> who would eventually found Assyria appear to have entered Mesopotamia at some point during the latter 4th millennium BC (c. 3500–3000 BC),<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> eventually intermingling with the earlier Sumerian-speaking population, who came from <a href="/wiki/Northern_Mesopotamia" class="mw-redirect" title="Northern Mesopotamia">northern Mesopotamia</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> with Akkadian names appearing in written record from as early as the 29th century BC.<sup id="cite_ref-Christopher_Woods_2008,_p.83_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Christopher_Woods_2008,_p.83-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the 3rd millennium BC, a very intimate cultural symbiosis developed between the <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerians</a> and the Akkadians throughout <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, which included widespread <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/bilingualism" class="extiw" title="wikt:bilingualism">bilingualism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Deutscher_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Deutscher-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> The influence of <a href="/wiki/Sumerian_language" title="Sumerian language">Sumerian</a> (a <a href="/wiki/Language_isolate" title="Language isolate">language isolate</a>) on Akkadian, and vice versa, is evident in all areas, from lexical borrowing on a massive scale, to syntactic, morphological, and phonological convergence.<sup id="cite_ref-Deutscher_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Deutscher-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> This has prompted scholars to refer to Sumerian and Akkadian in the third millennium BC as a <i><a href="/wiki/Sprachbund" title="Sprachbund">sprachbund</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Deutscher_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Deutscher-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Akkadian gradually replaced Sumerian as the spoken language of Mesopotamia somewhere after the turn of the 3rd and the 2nd millennium BC (the exact dating being a matter of debate),<sup id="cite_ref-woods_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-woods-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> although Sumerian continued to be used as a sacred, ceremonial, literary and scientific language in Mesopotamia until the 1st century AD, as did use of the <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_cuneiform" class="mw-redirect" title="Akkadian cuneiform">Akkadian cuneiform</a>.
</p><p>The cities of <a href="/wiki/Assur" title="Assur">Assur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nineveh" title="Nineveh">Nineveh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gasur" class="mw-redirect" title="Gasur">Gasur</a> and <a href="/wiki/Erbil" title="Erbil">Arbela</a> together with a number of other towns and cities, existed since at least before the middle of the 3rd millennium BC (c. 2600 BC), although they appear to have been Sumerian-ruled administrative centres at this time, rather than independent states.
</p><p>Greco-Roman classical writers such as <a href="/wiki/Julius_Africanus" title="Julius Africanus">Julius Africanus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Velleius_Paterculus" title="Marcus Velleius Paterculus">Marcus Velleius Paterculus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diodorus_Siculus" title="Diodorus Siculus">Diodorus Siculus</a> dated the founding of Assyria to various dates between 2284 BC and 2057 BC,<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> listing the earliest king as <a href="/wiki/Belus_(Assyrian)" title="Belus (Assyrian)">Belus</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ninus" title="Ninus">Ninus</a>.
</p><p>According to the Biblical <a href="/wiki/Generations_of_Noah" title="Generations of Noah">generations of Noah</a>, which appears to have been largely compiled between the 7th and 5th centuries BC,<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> the city of Aššur was allegedly founded by a biblical <a href="/wiki/Ashur_(Bible)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ashur (Bible)">Ashur</a> the son of <a href="/wiki/Shem" title="Shem">Shem</a>, who was deified by later generations as the city's patron god. However, the much older attested Assyrian tradition itself lists the first king of Assyria as the 25th century BC <a href="/wiki/Tudiya" title="Tudiya">Tudiya</a>, and an early urbanised Assyrian king named <a href="/wiki/Ushpia" title="Ushpia">Ushpia</a> (c. 2050 BC) as having dedicated the first temple to the god Ashur in the city in the mid-21st century BC. It is highly likely that the city was named in honour of its patron Assyrian god with the same name.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_Assyrian_Empire" title="Timeline of the Assyrian Empire">Timeline of the Assyrian Empire</a></div>
<h3><span id="Early_Period.2C_2600.E2.80.932025_BC"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Period,_2600–2025_BC">Early Period, 2600–2025 BC</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Early Period, 2600–2025 BC">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Early_Period_(Assyria)" title="Early Period (Assyria)">Early Period (Assyria)</a></div>
<table class="infobox geography vcard" style="width:22em;font-size:88%;"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" class="adr" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold;padding:0.25em 0.33em BC</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a href="/wiki/File:Second_Mariote_kingdom.png" class="image" title="A map detailing the location of Assyria within the Ancient Near East c. <td style="text-align:center;border:0; padding-bottom:0;"><b>Succeeded by</b>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td style="vertical-align:top; text-align:center; border:0;">

</td></tr>







































</tbody></table>
</td>
<td style="vertical-align:top; text-align:center;border:0;">
<table style="width:92%; background:transparent; text-align:center; margin:0 auto; border:0;">

<tbody><tr>
<td style="border:0; padding:0; vertical-align:middle; text-align:right;"><a href="/wiki/Old_Assyrian_Empire" title="Old Assyrian Empire">Old Assyrian Empire</a>
</td>
<td style="border:0; padding:0; vertical-align:middle;"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Samsi_Addu.PNG/20px-Samsi_Addu.PNG" decoding="async" width="20" height="16" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Samsi_Addu.PNG/30px-Samsi_Addu.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Samsi_Addu.PNG/40px-Samsi_Addu.PNG 2x" data-file-width="996" data-file-height="785" />
</td></tr>







































</tbody></table>
</td></tr></tbody></table></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Today part of</th><td><a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The city of <a href="/wiki/Assur" title="Assur">Aššur</a>, together with a number of other Assyrian cities, seem to have been established by 2600 BC. However it is likely that they were initially Sumerian-dominated administrative centres. In the late 26th century BC, <a href="/wiki/Eannatum" title="Eannatum">Eannatum</a> of <a href="/wiki/Lagash" title="Lagash">Lagash</a>, then the dominant <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerian</a> ruler in Mesopotamia, mentions "smiting <a href="/wiki/Subartu" title="Subartu">Subartu</a>" (Subartu being the Sumerian name for Assyria). Similarly, in c. the early 25th century BC, <a href="/wiki/Lugal-Anne-Mundu" title="Lugal-Anne-Mundu">Lugal-Anne-Mundu</a> the king of the Sumerian state of <a href="/wiki/Adab_(city)" title="Adab (city)">Adab</a> lists Subartu as paying tribute to him.
</p><p>Of the early history of the kingdom of Assyria, little is known. In the <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_King_List" class="mw-redirect" title="Assyrian King List">Assyrian King List</a>, the earliest king recorded was <a href="/wiki/Tudiya" title="Tudiya">Tudiya</a>. According to <a href="/wiki/Georges_Roux" title="Georges Roux">Georges Roux</a> he would have lived in the mid 25th century BC, i.e. <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;2450 BC</span>. In archaeological reports from <a href="/wiki/Ebla" title="Ebla">Ebla</a>, it appeared that Tudiya's activities were confirmed with the discovery of a tablet where he concluded a treaty for the operation of a <i><a href="/wiki/Karum_(trade_post)" title="Karum (trade post)">karum</a></i> (trading colony) in Eblaite territory, with "king" Ibrium of Ebla (who is now known to have been the <a href="/wiki/Vizier" title="Vizier">vizier</a> of Ebla for king Ishar-Damu).
</p><p>Tudiya was succeeded on the list by <a href="/wiki/Adamu_(Assyrian_king)" title="Adamu (Assyrian king)">Adamu</a>, the first known reference to the Semitic name <i>Adam</i><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> and then a further thirteen rulers (<a href="/wiki/Yangi" title="Yangi">Yangi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Suhlamu" title="Suhlamu">Suhlamu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harharu" title="Harharu">Harharu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mandaru" title="Mandaru">Mandaru</a>, <a href="/wiki/Imsu" title="Imsu">Imsu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harsu" title="Harsu">Harsu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Didanu" title="Didanu">Didanu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hanu" class="mw-redirect" title="Hanu">Hanu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zuabu" title="Zuabu">Zuabu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuabu" title="Nuabu">Nuabu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abazu_(Assyrian_king)" title="Abazu (Assyrian king)">Abazu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belus_(Assyrian)" title="Belus (Assyrian)">Belus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Azarah" title="Azarah">Azarah</a>). Nothing concrete is yet known about these names, although it has been noted that a much later Babylonian tablet listing the ancestral lineage of <a href="/wiki/Hammurabi" title="Hammurabi">Hammurabi</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Amorite" class="mw-redirect" title="Amorite">Amorite</a> king of Babylon, seems to have copied the same names from Tudiya through Nuabu, though in a heavily corrupted form.
</p><p>The earliest kings, such as Tudiya, who are recorded as <i>kings who lived in tents</i>, were independent semi-nomadic pastoralist rulers. These kings at some point became fully urbanised and founded the <i>city state</i> of <a href="/wiki/A%C5%A1%C5%A1ur" class="mw-redirect" title="Aššur">Aššur</a> in the mid 21st-century BC.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESaggs198424_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESaggs198424-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Akkadian_Empire_and_Neo-Sumerian_Empires.2C_2334.E2.80.932050_BC"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Akkadian_Empire_and_Neo-Sumerian_Empires,_2334–2050_BC">Akkadian Empire and Neo-Sumerian Empires, 2334–2050 BC</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Akkadian Empire and Neo-Sumerian Empires, 2334–2050 BC">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_Empire" title="Akkadian Empire">Akkadian Empire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neo-Sumerian_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Neo-Sumerian Empire">Neo-Sumerian Empire</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Empire_akkad.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Empire_akkad.svg/250px-Empire_akkad.svg.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="195" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Empire_akkad.svg/375px-Empire_akkad.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Empire_akkad.svg/500px-Empire_akkad.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1475" data-file-height="1149" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Empire_akkad.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of the Akkadian Empire (brown) and the directions in which military campaigns were conducted (yellow arrows).</div></div></div>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_Empire" title="Akkadian Empire">Akkadian Empire</a> (2334–2154 BC), the Assyrians, like all the Akkadian-speaking Mesopotamians (and also the Sumerians), became subject to the dynasty of the city-state of <a href="/wiki/Akkad_(city)" title="Akkad (city)">Akkad</a>, centered in central Mesopotamia. The Akkadian Empire founded by <a href="/wiki/Sargon_the_Great" class="mw-redirect" title="Sargon the Great">Sargon the Great</a> claimed to encompass the surrounding "four quarters". The region of Assyria, north of the seat of the empire in central Mesopotamia, had also been known as <a href="/wiki/Subartu" title="Subartu">Subartu</a> by the Sumerians, and the name Azuhinum in Akkadian records also seems to refer to Assyria proper.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> The Sumerians were eventually absorbed into the Akkadian (Assyro-Babylonian) population.<sup id="cite_ref-Deutscher_29-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Deutscher-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-woods_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-woods-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Assyrian rulers were subject to Sargon and his successors, and the city of Ashur became a regional administrative center of the Empire, implicated by the <a href="/wiki/Nuzi#Archaeology" title="Nuzi">Nuzi tablets</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Shendge1997_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shendge1997-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> During this period, the Akkadian-speaking Semites of Mesopotamia came to rule an empire encompassing not only Mesopotamia itself but large swathes of <a href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>, ancient Iran, Elam, the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a>, Canaan and Syria.
</p><p>Assyria seems to have already been firmly involved in trade in Asia Minor by this time; the earliest known reference to Anatolian <i><a href="/wiki/Karum_(trade_post)" title="Karum (trade post)">karu</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/Hittite_empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Hittite empire">Hatti</a> was found on later cuneiform tablets describing the early period of the Akkadian Empire (c. 2350 BC). On those tablets, Assyrian traders in <a href="/wiki/Burushanda" class="mw-redirect" title="Burushanda">Burushanda</a> implored the help of their ruler, Sargon the Great, and this appellation continued to exist throughout the Assyrian Empire for about 1,700 years. The name "Hatti" itself even appears in later accounts of his grandson, <a href="/wiki/Naram-Sin_of_Akkad" title="Naram-Sin of Akkad">Naram-Sin</a>, campaigning in <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a>.
</p><p>Assyrian and Akkadian traders spread the use of writing in the form of the Mesopotamian <a href="/wiki/Cuneiform" title="Cuneiform">cuneiform</a> script to Asia Minor and <a href="/wiki/The_Levant" class="mw-redirect" title="The Levant">the Levant</a> (modern <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a>). However, towards the end of the reign of <a href="/wiki/Sargon_the_Great" class="mw-redirect" title="Sargon the Great">Sargon the Great</a>, the Assyrian faction rebelled against him; "the tribes of Assyria of the upper country—in their turn attacked, but they submitted to his arms, and Sargon settled their habitations, and he smote them grievously".<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Ur_III.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Map_of_Ur_III.svg/250px-Map_of_Ur_III.svg.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="286" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Map_of_Ur_III.svg/375px-Map_of_Ur_III.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Map_of_Ur_III.svg/500px-Map_of_Ur_III.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="350" data-file-height="400" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Ur_III.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Empire of the Third Dynasty of Ur. West is at top, North at right.</div></div></div>
<p>The Akkadian Empire was destroyed by economic decline and internal civil war, followed by attacks from barbarian <a href="/wiki/Gutian_people" title="Gutian people">Gutian people</a> in 2154 BC. The rulers of Assyria during the period between c. 2154 BC and 2112 BC once again became fully independent, as the Gutians are only known to have administered southern Mesopotamia. However, the king list is the only information from Assyria for this period.
</p><p>Most of Assyria briefly became part of the <a href="/wiki/Neo-Sumerian_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Neo-Sumerian Empire">Neo-Sumerian Empire</a> (or <a href="/wiki/3rd_dynasty_of_Ur" class="mw-redirect" title="3rd dynasty of Ur">3rd dynasty of Ur</a>) founded in c. 2112 BC. Sumerian domination extended as far as the city of Ashur but appears not to have reached Nineveh and the far north of Assyria. One local ruler (<i>shakkanakku</i>) named Zāriqum (who does not appear on any Assyrian king list) is listed as paying tribute to <a href="/wiki/Amar-Sin" title="Amar-Sin">Amar-Sin</a> of Ur. Ashur's rulers appear to have remained largely under Sumerian domination until the mid-21st century BC (c. 2050 BC); the king list names Assyrian rulers for this period and several are known from other references to have also borne the title of <i>shakkanakka</i> or vassal governors for the neo-Sumerians.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Old_Assyrian_Empire.2C_2025.E2.80.931522_BC"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Old_Assyrian_Empire,_2025–1522_BC">Old Assyrian Empire, 2025–1522 BC</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Old Assyrian Empire, 2025–1522 BC">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Old_Assyrian_Empire" title="Old Assyrian Empire">Old Assyrian Empire</a></div>

<td style="text-align:center; border:0; padding-bottom:0"><div id="before-after"></div> <b>Preceded by</b></td>

</td></tr>
<tr>
<td style="vertical-align:top; text-align:center; border:0;">
<table style="width:100%; background: transparent; text-align:center; margin:0 auto; border:0;">

<tbody><tr>
<td style="border:0; padding:0; vertical-align:middle;"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/d/d2/Blank.png" decoding="async" width="22" height="15" data-file-width="3" data-file-height="2" />
</td>
<td style="border:0; padding:0; vertical-align:middle; text-align:left;"><a href="/wiki/Early_Assyrian_kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Assyrian kingdom">Early Assyrian kingdom</a>

<tbody><tr>
<td style="border:0; padding:0; vertical-align:middle; text-align:right;"><a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Mitanni" class="mw-redirect" title="Kingdom of Mitanni">Kingdom of Mitanni</a>
</td>
<td style="border:0; padding:0; vertical-align:middle;"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/d/d2/Blank.png" decoding="async" width="22" height="15" data-file-width="3" data-file-height="2" />
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td style="border:0; padding:0; vertical-align:middle; text-align:right;"><a href="/wiki/Middle_Assyrian_Empire" title="Middle Assyrian Empire">Middle Assyrian Empire</a>
</td>
<td style="border:0; padding:0; vertical-align:middle;"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/d/d2/Blank.png" decoding="async" width="22" height="15" data-file-width="3" data-file-height="2" />
</td></tr>





































</tbody></table>
</td></tr></tbody></table></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Today part of</th><td>Iraq, Syria and Turkey</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Old_Assyrian_Empire" title="Old Assyrian Empire">Old Assyrian Empire</a> is one of four periods into which the history of Assyria is divided, the other three being: the <a href="/wiki/Early_Period_(Assyria)" title="Early Period (Assyria)">Early Assyrian Period</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Assyrian_Empire" title="Middle Assyrian Empire">Middle Assyrian Period</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">New Assyrian Period</a>. 
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ushpia" title="Ushpia">Ushpia</a> (2080 BC) appears to have been the first fully urbanised independent king of Assyria, and is traditionally held to have dedicated temples to the god <a href="/wiki/Ashur" title="Ashur">Ashur</a> in the city of the same name.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> He was followed by <a href="/wiki/Sulili" title="Sulili">Sulili</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kikkiya" class="mw-redirect" title="Kikkiya">Kikkiya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Akiya" title="Akiya">Akiya</a>, of whom little is known aside from Kikkiya conducting various building works in Assur. A number of scholars also place <a href="/wiki/Zariqum" title="Zariqum">Zariqum</a>, a contemporary of <a href="/wiki/Amar-Sin" title="Amar-Sin">Amar-Sin</a> (2046 – 2038 BC) of <a href="/wiki/Ur" title="Ur">Ur</a> as an Assyrian ruler, though he does not appear on the Assyrian king list, but is claimed by Amar-Sin to be the 'governor' of Assur<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>.
</p><p>
In approximately 2025 BC, a king named <a href="/wiki/Puzur-Ashur_I" title="Puzur-Ashur I">Puzur-Ashur I</a> came to the throne of Assyria, and there is some debate among scholars as to whether he was the founder of a new dynasty or a descendant of Ushpia. He is mentioned as having conducted building projects in Assur, and he and his successors took the title <i>Išši’ak Aššur</i> (meaning viceroy of Ashur). From this time Assyria began to expand trading colonies called <a href="/wiki/Karum_(trade_post)" title="Karum (trade post)">Karum</a> into <a href="/wiki/Hurrian" class="mw-redirect" title="Hurrian">Hurrian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hattians" title="Hattians">Hattian</a> lands in <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>. He was succeeded by <a href="/wiki/Shalim-ahum" title="Shalim-ahum">Shalim-ahum</a> (c. 2000 BC), a king who is attested in a contemporary record of the time, leaving inscriptions in an archaic form of Akkadian<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>. In addition to the expansions into Anatolia <a href="/wiki/Ilu-shuma" title="Ilu-shuma">Ilu-shuma</a> (C. 1995-1974 BC) (<a href="/wiki/Middle_chronology" title="Middle chronology">Middle chronology</a>) appears to have conducted military campaigns in southern Mesopotamia, either in conquest of the city-states of the south, or in order to protect his fellow Akkadian-speakers from incursions by <a href="/wiki/Elamites" class="mw-redirect" title="Elamites">Elamites</a> from the east and/or <a href="/wiki/Amorites" title="Amorites">Amorites</a> from the west - <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style></p><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>"The freedom<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;nb 1&#93;</a></sup> of the Akkadians and their children I established. I purified their copper. I established their freedom from the border of the marshes and <a href="/wiki/Ur" title="Ur">Ur</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nippur" title="Nippur">Nippur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Awal" title="Awal">Awal</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kismar" title="Kismar">Kismar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Der_(Sumer)" title="Der (Sumer)">Der</a> of the god <a href="/wiki/Ishtaran" class="mw-redirect" title="Ishtaran">Ishtaran</a>, as far as Assur."<sup id="cite_ref-ari_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ari-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>7–8</span></sup>
</p></blockquote><p> He is known to have built the old temple of <a href="/wiki/Ishtar" class="mw-redirect" title="Ishtar">Ishtar</a> in Assur. He was succeeded by another powerful king, the long reigning <a href="/wiki/Erishum_I" title="Erishum I">Erishum I</a> (1973-1934 BC) who is notable for one of the earliest examples of written <a href="/wiki/Legal_codes" class="mw-redirect" title="Legal codes">legal codes</a><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> and introducing the <a href="/wiki/Limmu" title="Limmu">limmu</a> (eponym) lists that were to continue throughout Assyrian history. He is known to have greatly expanded Assyrian trading colonies in Anatolia, with twenty one being listed during his reign. These Karum traded in: tin, textiles, lapis lazuli, iron, antimony, copper, bronze, wool, and grain, in exchange for gold and silver. Erishum also kept numerous written records, and conducted major building works in Assyria, including the building of temples to Ashur, Ishtar and <a href="/wiki/Adad" class="mw-redirect" title="Adad">Adad</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>These policies were continued by <a href="/wiki/Ikunum" title="Ikunum">Ikunum</a> (1933–1921), <a href="/wiki/Sargon_I" title="Sargon I">Sargon I</a> (1920–1881 BC), likely named after his predecessor <a href="/wiki/Sargon_of_Akkad" title="Sargon of Akkad">Sargon of Akkad</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> (during Sargon I's later reign <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a> was founded as a small city-state), and <a href="/wiki/Puzur-Ashur_II" title="Puzur-Ashur II">Puzur-Ashur II</a> (1880–1873 BC). <a href="/wiki/Naram-Sin_of_Assyria" title="Naram-Sin of Assyria">Naram-Sin</a> (1872–1828 BC) repelled an attempted usurpation of his throne by the future king <a href="/wiki/Shamshi-Adad_I" title="Shamshi-Adad I">Shamshi-Adad I</a> late in his reign, however his successor <a href="/wiki/Erishum_II" title="Erishum II">Erishum II</a> was deposed by Shamshi-Adad I in 1809 BC, bringing an end to the dynasty founded either by Ushpia or Puzur-Ashur I.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Shamshi-Adad_I" title="Shamshi-Adad I">Shamshi-Adad I</a> (1808-1776 BC) was already the ruler of <a href="/wiki/Terqa" title="Terqa">Terqa</a>, and although he claimed Assyrian ancestry as a descendant of Ushpia, he is regarded as a foreign <a href="/wiki/Amorite" class="mw-redirect" title="Amorite">Amorite</a> usurper by later Assyrian tradition. However, he greatly expanded the <a href="/wiki/Old_Assyrian_Empire" title="Old Assyrian Empire">Old Assyrian Empire</a>, incorporating the northern half of Mesopotamia, swathes of eastern and southern Anatolia and much of the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a> into his large empire, and campaigned as far west as the eastern shores of the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean" class="mw-redirect" title="Mediterranean">Mediterranean</a><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>. His son and successor <a href="/wiki/Ishme-Dagan_I" title="Ishme-Dagan I">Ishme-Dagan I</a> (1775-1764 BC) gradually lost territory in southern Mesopotamia and the Levant to the state of <a href="/wiki/Mari,_Syria" title="Mari, Syria">Mari</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eshnunna" title="Eshnunna">Eshnunna</a> respectively, and had mixed relations with <a href="/wiki/Hammurabi" title="Hammurabi">Hammurabi</a>, the king who had turned the hitherto young and insignificant city-state of <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a> into a major power and empire.
</p><p>After Shamsi-Adad I's death Assyria was reduced to vassalage by Hammurabi; <a href="/wiki/Mut-Ashkur" title="Mut-Ashkur">Mut-Ashkur</a> (1763-1753), <a href="/wiki/Rimush" title="Rimush">Rimush</a> and <a href="/wiki/Asinum" title="Asinum">Asinum</a> were subservient to Hammurabi, who also took ownership of Assyrian trading colonies, thus bringing an end to the Old Assyrian Empire.
</p><p>However, the Babylonian empire proved to be short lived, rapidly collapsing after the death of Hammurabi c. 1750 BC. An Assyrian governor named <a href="/wiki/Puzur-Sin" title="Puzur-Sin">Puzur-Sin</a> deposed Asinum who was regarded as a foreign Amorite and a puppet of the new and ineffectual Babylonian king <a href="/wiki/Sumuabum" class="mw-redirect" title="Sumuabum">Sumuabum</a>, and the Babylonian and Amorite presence was expunged from Assyria by Puzur-Sin and his successor <a href="/wiki/Ashur-dugul" title="Ashur-dugul">Ashur-dugul</a>, who reigned for six years. A king called <a href="/wiki/Adasi" title="Adasi">Adasi</a> (1720-1701) finally restored strength and stability to Assyria, ending the civil unrest that had followed the ejection of the Babylonians and Amorites, founding the new <i>Adaside Dynasty</i><sup id="cite_ref-auto_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>. <a href="/wiki/Bel-bani" title="Bel-bani">Bel-bani</a> (1700 -1691 BC) succeeded Adasi and further strengthened Assyria against potential threats<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>, and remained a revered figure even in the time of <a href="/wiki/Ashurbanipal" title="Ashurbanipal">Ashurbanipal</a> over a thousand years later<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>.
</p><p>There followed a long, prosperous and peaceful period in Assyrian history, rulers such as <a href="/wiki/Libaya" title="Libaya">Libaya</a> (1691–1674 BC), <a href="/wiki/Sharma-Adad_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Sharma-Adad I">Sharma-Adad I</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iptar-Sin" title="Iptar-Sin">Iptar-Sin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bazaya" title="Bazaya">Bazaya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lullaya" title="Lullaya">Lullaya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shu-Ninua" title="Shu-Ninua">Shu-Ninua</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sharma-Adad_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Sharma-Adad II">Sharma-Adad II</a> appear to have had peaceful and largely uneventful reigns<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Assyria remained strong and secure; when Babylon was sacked and its Amorite rulers deposed by the <a href="/wiki/Hittite_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Hittite Empire">Hittite Empire</a> and subsequently fell to the <a href="/wiki/Kassites" title="Kassites">Kassites</a> in 1595 BC, both powers were unable to make any inroads into Assyria, and there seems to have been no trouble between the first Kassite ruler of Babylon, <a href="/wiki/Agum_II" title="Agum II">Agum II</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Erishum_III" class="mw-redirect" title="Erishum III">Erishum III</a> (1598–1586 BC) of Assyria, and a mutually beneficial treaty was signed between the two rulers. <a href="/wiki/Shamshi-Adad_II" title="Shamshi-Adad II">Shamshi-Adad II</a> (1585–1580 BC), <a href="/wiki/Ishme-Dagan_II" title="Ishme-Dagan II">Ishme-Dagan II</a> (1579–1562 BC) and <a href="/wiki/Shamshi-Adad_III" title="Shamshi-Adad III">Shamshi-Adad III</a> (1562–1548 BC) seem also to have had peaceful tenures, although few records have thus far been discovered about their reigns. Similarly, <a href="/wiki/Ashur-nirari_I" title="Ashur-nirari I">Ashur-nirari I</a> (1547–1522 BC) seems not to have been troubled by the newly founded <a href="/wiki/Mitanni" title="Mitanni">Mitanni</a> Empire in Asia Minor, the Hittite empire, or Babylon during his 25-year reign. He, and his successor <a href="/wiki/Puzur-Ashur_III" title="Puzur-Ashur III">Puzur-Ashur III</a> (1521-1497 BC) are known to have been an active kings, improving the infrastructure, dedicating temples and conducting various building projects throughout the kingdom. <a href="/wiki/Enlil-nasir_I" title="Enlil-nasir I">Enlil-nasir I</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nur-ili" title="Nur-ili">Nur-ili</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ashur-shaduni" title="Ashur-shaduni">Ashur-shaduni</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ashur-rabi_I" title="Ashur-rabi I">Ashur-rabi I</a> (who deposed his predecessor) followed<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>.
</p>
<h4><span id="Decline.2C_1450.E2.80.931393_BC"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Decline,_1450–1393_BC">Decline, 1450–1393 BC</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Decline, 1450–1393 BC">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The emergence of the Hurri-Mitanni Empire and allied Hittite empire in the 16th century BC did eventually lead to a short period of sporadic <a href="/wiki/Mitannian" class="mw-redirect" title="Mitannian">Mitannian</a>-<a href="/wiki/Hurrian" class="mw-redirect" title="Hurrian">Hurrian</a> domination in the latter half of the 15th century. The Mitannians (an <a href="/wiki/Indo-Aryan_languages" title="Indo-Aryan languages">Indo-Aryan</a> speaking people) are thought to have entered Anatolia from the north, conquered and formed the ruling class over the indigenous Hurrians of eastern Anatolia. The indigenous Hurrians spoke the <a href="/wiki/Hurrian_language" title="Hurrian language">Hurrian language</a>, a language in the now wholly extinct <a href="/wiki/Hurro-Urartian" class="mw-redirect" title="Hurro-Urartian">Hurro-Urartian</a> language family.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ashur-nadin-ahhe_I" title="Ashur-nadin-ahhe I">Ashur-nadin-ahhe I</a> (1450–1431 BC) was courted by the <a href="/wiki/Egyptians" title="Egyptians">Egyptians</a>, who were rivals of Mitanni, and attempting to gain a foothold in the Near East. <a href="/wiki/Amenhotep_II" title="Amenhotep II">Amenhotep II</a> sent the Assyrian king a tribute of gold to seal an alliance against the Hurri-Mitannian empire. It is likely that this alliance prompted <a href="/wiki/Saushtatar" class="mw-redirect" title="Saushtatar">Saushtatar</a>, the emperor of Mitanni, to invade Assyria, and sack the city of Ashur, after which Assyria became a sometime vassal state. Ashur-nadin-ahhe I was deposed, either by Shaustatar or by his own brother <a href="/wiki/Enlil-nasir_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Enlil-nasir II">Enlil-nasir II</a> (1430–1425 BC) in 1430 BC, who then paid tribute to the Mitanni. <a href="/wiki/Ashur-nirari_II" title="Ashur-nirari II">Ashur-nirari II</a> (1424–1418 BC) had an uneventful reign and appears to have also paid tribute to the Mitanni Empire.
</p><p>The Assyrian monarchy survived, and the Mitannian influence appears to have been short-lived.
</p><p>They appear not to have been always willing or indeed able to interfere in Assyrian internal and international affairs.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ashur-bel-nisheshu" title="Ashur-bel-nisheshu">Ashur-bel-nisheshu</a> (1417–1409 BC) seems to have been independent of Mitannian influence, as evidenced by his signing a mutually beneficial treaty with <a href="/wiki/Karaindash" title="Karaindash">Karaindash</a>, the Kassite king of Babylonia in the late 15th century. He also undertook extensive rebuilding work in Ashur itself, and Assyria appears to have redeveloped its former highly sophisticated financial and economic systems during his reign. <a href="/wiki/Ashur-rim-nisheshu" title="Ashur-rim-nisheshu">Ashur-rim-nisheshu</a> (1408–1401 BC) also undertook building work, strengthening the city walls of the capital. <a href="/wiki/Ashur-nadin-ahhe_II" title="Ashur-nadin-ahhe II">Ashur-nadin-ahhe II</a> (1400–1393 BC) also received a tribute of gold and diplomatic overtures from Egypt, probably in an attempt to gain Assyrian military support against Egypt's Mitannian and Hittite rivals in the region. However, the Assyrian king appears not to have been in a strong enough position to challenge Mitanni or the Hittites.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Eriba-Adad_I" title="Eriba-Adad I">Eriba-Adad I</a> (1392–1366 BC), a son of Ashur-bel-nisheshu, ascended the throne in 1392 BC and finally broke the ties to the Mitanni Empire, and instead turned the tables, and began to exert Assyrian influence on the Mitanni.
</p>
<h3><span id="Middle_Assyrian_Empire_1392.E2.80.931056_BC"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Assyrian_Empire_1392–1056_BC">Middle Assyrian Empire 1392–1056 BC</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Middle Assyrian Empire 1392–1056 BC">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Middle_Assyrian_Empire" title="Middle Assyrian Empire">Middle Assyrian Empire</a></div>

</td>
<td style="border:0; padding:0; vertical-align:middle;"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Map_of_Assyria.png/20px-Map_of_Assyria.png" decoding="async" width="20" height="14" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Map_of_Assyria.png/30px-Map_of_Assyria.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Map_of_Assyria.png/40px-Map_of_Assyria.png 2x" data-file-width="1983" data-file-height="1363" />
