</td></tr></tbody></table></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:M%C3%A9dio-assyrien.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/M%C3%A9dio-assyrien.png/250px-M%C3%A9dio-assyrien.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="205" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/M%C3%A9dio-assyrien.png/375px-M%C3%A9dio-assyrien.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/M%C3%A9dio-assyrien.png/500px-M%C3%A9dio-assyrien.png 2x" data-file-width="883" data-file-height="724" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:M%C3%A9dio-assyrien.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Near_East" title="Ancient Near East">Ancient Near East</a> showing the extent of the <b>Middle Assyrian Empire</b> (orange) <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;1392 BC</span>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mesopotamia_1200_BC.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Mesopotamia_1200_BC.jpg/250px-Mesopotamia_1200_BC.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Mesopotamia_1200_BC.jpg/375px-Mesopotamia_1200_BC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Mesopotamia_1200_BC.jpg/500px-Mesopotamia_1200_BC.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2256" data-file-height="1555" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mesopotamia_1200_BC.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mesopotamia and Middle Assyrian Empire, c. 1200 BC.</div></div></div>
<p>The Middle period (1365 BC–1056 BC) saw reigns of great kings, such as <a href="/wiki/Ashur-uballit_I" title="Ashur-uballit I">Ashur-uballit I</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arik-den-ili" title="Arik-den-ili">Arik-den-ili</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tukulti-Ninurta_I" title="Tukulti-Ninurta I">Tukulti-Ninurta I</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tiglath-Pileser_I" title="Tiglath-Pileser I">Tiglath-Pileser I</a>. During this period, Assyria overthrew the empire of the <a href="/wiki/Hurri" class="mw-redirect" title="Hurri">Hurri</a>-<a href="/wiki/Mitanni" title="Mitanni">Mitanni</a> and eclipsed the <a href="/wiki/Hittite_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Hittite Empire">Hittite Empire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Egyptian Empire">Egyptian Empire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elam" title="Elam">Elam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canaan" title="Canaan">Canaan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phrygia" title="Phrygia">Phrygia</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Assyrian_troops_return.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Assyrian_troops_return.png/220px-Assyrian_troops_return.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="93" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Assyrian_troops_return.png/330px-Assyrian_troops_return.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Assyrian_troops_return.png/440px-Assyrian_troops_return.png 2x" data-file-width="942" data-file-height="400" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Assyrian_troops_return.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Assyrian troops return after victory.</div></div></div>
<p>By the reign of <a href="/wiki/Eriba-Adad_I" title="Eriba-Adad I">Eriba-Adad I</a> (1392–1366 BC) <a href="/wiki/Mitanni" title="Mitanni">Mitanni</a> influence over Assyria was on the wane. Eriba-Adad I became involved in a dynastic battle between <a href="/wiki/Tushratta" title="Tushratta">Tushratta</a> and his brother <a href="/wiki/Artatama_II" title="Artatama II">Artatama II</a> and after this his son <a href="/wiki/Shuttarna_III" title="Shuttarna III">Shuttarna III</a>, who called himself king of the Hurri while seeking support from the Assyrians. 
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ashur-uballit_I" title="Ashur-uballit I">Ashur-uballit I</a> (1365-1330 BC) went further, defeating Shuttarna III and bringing an end to the Mitanni empire, the Assyrian king then annexing its territories in Anatolia and the Levant, turning Assyria once more into a major empire<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>. The ambitious Assyrian king went further still, attacking and conquering <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonia</a>, and imposing a puppet ruler loyal to himself upon its throne. Assyria then annexed hitherto Babylonian territory in central Mesopotamia<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>. <a href="/wiki/Enlil-nirari" title="Enlil-nirari">Enlil-nirari</a> (1330-1319 BC) also defeated Babylonia's Kassite kings.
</p><p>The Hittites, having failed to save Mitanni, allied with Babylon in an unsuccessful economic war against Assyria for many years. Assyria was now a large and powerful empire, and a major threat to Egyptian and Hittite interests in the region, and was perhaps the reason that these two powers, fearful of Assyrian might, made peace with one another.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Arik-den-ili" title="Arik-den-ili">Arik-den-ili</a> (1318-1307 BC) campaigned further still, entering northern <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Iran" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Iran">Ancient Iran</a> and subjugating the 'pre-Iranic' Gutians, Turukku and Nigimhi, before campaining deeper into the Levant, subjugating the Suteans, Ahlamu and Yauru<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>. His successor <a href="/wiki/Adad-nirari_I" title="Adad-nirari I">Adad-nirari I</a> (1307 -1275 BC) was another highly successful military leader, he defeated and conquered the Hurro-Mitanni kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Hanigalbat" class="mw-redirect" title="Hanigalbat">Hanigalbat</a> and the rest of the independent Hurro-Mitanni kingdoms of Anatolia, despite the Hittites attempting to support their allies, and inflicted a series of crushing defeats on Babylonia, annexing large swathes of Babylonian territory. Hittite kings during his reign assumed a placatory attitude towards the Assyrian king<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Shalmaneser_I" title="Shalmaneser I">Shalmaneser I</a> (1274-1245 BC) conquered eight kingdoms in central Anatolia in his first year, and in the next he defeated a coalition of Hittites, Hurrians, Mitanni and Ahlamu, annexing yet more territory in Anatolia and the Levant, and retaining Assyrian dominion over Babylonia and the northwest of ancient Iran. Shalmaneser also conducted extensive building work in <a href="/wiki/Assur" title="Assur">Assur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nineveh" title="Nineveh">Nineveh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Erbil" title="Erbil">Arbela</a>, and founded the city of <a href="/wiki/Kalhu" class="mw-redirect" title="Kalhu">Kalhu</a> (the Biblical <a href="/wiki/Calah" class="mw-redirect" title="Calah">Calah</a>/<a href="/wiki/Nimrud" title="Nimrud">Nimrud</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>.
</p><p>Shalmaneser's son and successor, <a href="/wiki/Tukulti-Ninurta_I" title="Tukulti-Ninurta I">Tukulti-Ninurta I</a> (1244–1207 BC), won a major victory against the Hittites and their king <a href="/wiki/Tudhaliya_IV" class="mw-redirect" title="Tudhaliya IV">Tudhaliya IV</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Nihriya" title="Battle of Nihriya">Battle of Nihriya</a> and took thousands of prisoners. Rather than being content to simply subjugate Babylonian kings as his predecessors had, he conquered Babylonia directly, taking <a href="/wiki/Kashtiliash_IV" title="Kashtiliash IV">Kashtiliash IV</a> as a captive and ruled there himself as king for seven years, taking on the old title "King of Sumer and Akkad" first used by Sargon of Akkad. Tukulti-Ninurta I thus became the first Akkadian speaking native Mesopotamian to rule the state of Babylonia, its founders having been foreign Amorites, succeeded by equally foreign Kassites. Tukulti-Ninurta petitioned the god <a href="/wiki/Shamash" class="mw-redirect" title="Shamash">Shamash</a> before beginning his counter offensive.<sup id="cite_ref-edwards2_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-edwards2-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kashtiliash_IV" title="Kashtiliash IV">Kashtiliash IV</a> was captured, single-handed by Tukulti-Ninurta according to <i>his</i> account, who "trod with my feet upon his lordly neck as though it were a footstool"<sup id="cite_ref-Albert_Kirk_Grayson_1972_108_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Albert_Kirk_Grayson_1972_108-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> and deported him ignominiously in chains to Assyria. The victorious Assyrians demolished the walls of Babylon, massacred many of the inhabitants, pillaged and plundered his way across the city to the <a href="/wiki/Esagila" title="Esagila">Esagila</a> temple, where he made off with the statue of <a href="/wiki/Marduk" title="Marduk">Marduk</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Middle Assyrian texts recovered at ancient <a href="/wiki/D%C5%ABr-Katlimmu" class="mw-redirect" title="Dūr-Katlimmu">Dūr-Katlimmu</a>, include a letter from Tukulti-Ninurta to his <i>sukkal rabi'u</i>, or grand vizier, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ashur-iddin&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Ashur-iddin (page does not exist)">Ashur-iddin</a> advising him of the approach of his general <a href="/w/index.php?title=Shulman-mushabshu&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Shulman-mushabshu (page does not exist)">Shulman-mushabshu</a> escorting the captive Kashtiliash, his wife, and his retinue which incorporated a large number of women,<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> on his way to exile after his defeat. In the process he defeated the <a href="/wiki/Elamites" class="mw-redirect" title="Elamites">Elamites</a>, who had themselves coveted Babylon. He also wrote an epic poem documenting his victorious wars against Babylon and Elam. He progressed further south into what is today <a href="/wiki/Arabia" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabia">Arabia</a>, conquering the <i>pre-Arab</i> <a href="/wiki/South_Semitic" class="mw-redirect" title="South Semitic">South Semitic</a> kingdoms of <a href="/wiki/Dilmun" title="Dilmun">Dilmun</a> and <a href="/wiki/Meluhha" title="Meluhha">Meluhha</a>. After a Babylonian revolt, he raided and plundered the temples in Babylon, regarded as an act of sacrilege. As relations with the priesthood in Ashur began deteriorating, Tukulti-Ninurta built a new capital city; <a href="/wiki/Kar-Tukulti-Ninurta" title="Kar-Tukulti-Ninurta">Kar-Tukulti-Ninurta</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>A series of short reigning kings followed, these being <a href="/wiki/Ashur-nadin-apli" title="Ashur-nadin-apli">Ashur-nadin-apli</a> (1207 -1204 BC), <a href="/wiki/Ashur-nirari_III" title="Ashur-nirari III">Ashur-nirari III</a> (1203-1198 BC), <a href="/wiki/Enlil-kudurri-usur" title="Enlil-kudurri-usur">Enlil-kudurri-usur</a> (1197-1193 BC) and <a href="/wiki/Ninurta-apal-Ekur" title="Ninurta-apal-Ekur">Ninurta-apal-Ekur</a> (1192-1190 BC), and there were no significant expansions of the empire during their short tenures, and Babylonia seems to have freed itself from the Assyrian yoke for a time.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ashur-dan_I" title="Ashur-dan I">Ashur-dan I</a> (1190-1144 BC) conquered huge swathes of Babylonia, subjugating its king, and taking much booty home to Assyria. However, this led to conflict with the powerful <a href="/wiki/Elamites" class="mw-redirect" title="Elamites">Elamites</a> of the southwest of ancient Iran, who were themselves preying upon Babylonia. The Elamites managed to actually take the Assyrian city of <a href="/wiki/Arrapha" title="Arrapha">Arrapha</a> (modern <a href="/wiki/Kirkuk" title="Kirkuk">Kirkuk</a>), before being finally defeated and driven from the Assyrian empire<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>. Civil unrest ensued in Assyria after Ashur-Dan I's death, and <a href="/wiki/Ninurta-tukulti-Ashur" title="Ninurta-tukulti-Ashur">Ninurta-tukulti-Ashur</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mutakkil-Nusku" title="Mutakkil-Nusku">Mutakkil-Nusku</a> followed in quick succession during 1133 BC.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ashur-resh-ishi_I" title="Ashur-resh-ishi I">Ashur-resh-ishi I</a> (1133-1116 BC) restored the tradition of powerful conquering kings. He campaigned to the east, taking the <a href="/wiki/Zagros" class="mw-redirect" title="Zagros">Zagros</a> region of ancient Iran, and subjugated the Amorites, Ahlamu and the newly appeared Arameans in the Levant. He also defeated the ambitious <a href="/wiki/Nebuchadnezzar_I" title="Nebuchadnezzar I">Nebuchadnezzar I</a> of Babylonia, annexing Babylonian territory in the process<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Tiglath-pileser_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Tiglath-pileser I">Tiglath-pileser I</a> (1115-1074 BC) proved to be a long reigning and all conquering ruler, who firmly underlined Assyria's position as the world's leading military power<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>.
</p><p>His first campaign was against the <a href="/wiki/Phrygians" title="Phrygians">Phrygians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kaskians" title="Kaskians">Kaskians</a> in 1112 BC, who had attemted to occupy certain Assyrian ruled Hittite districts in the Upper <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a>; then he overran <a href="/wiki/Commagene" class="mw-redirect" title="Commagene">Commagene</a> and eastern <a href="/wiki/Cappadocia" title="Cappadocia">Cappadocia</a>, and drove the <a href="/wiki/Hittites" title="Hittites">Hittites</a> from the Assyrian province of <a href="/wiki/Subartu" title="Subartu">Subartu</a>, northeast of <a href="/wiki/Malatia" class="mw-redirect" title="Malatia">Malatia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911968_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911968-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In a subsequent campaign, the Assyrian forces penetrated into the mountains south of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Van" title="Lake Van">Lake Van</a> and then turned westward to receive the submission of <a href="/wiki/Malatia" class="mw-redirect" title="Malatia">Malatia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Urartu" title="Urartu">Urartu</a>. In his fifth year, Tiglath-Pileser attacked <a href="/wiki/Cilicia" title="Cilicia">Cilicia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Comana_(Cappadocia)" title="Comana (Cappadocia)">Comana</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cappadocia" title="Cappadocia">Cappadocia</a>, and placed a record of his victories engraved on copper plates in a fortress he built to secure his <a href="/wiki/Cilicia" title="Cilicia">Cilician</a> conquests.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911968_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911968-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Aramaeans" class="mw-redirect" title="Aramaeans">Aramaeans</a> of northern and central Syria were the next targets of the Assyrian king, who made his way as far as the sources of the Tigris.<sup id="cite_ref-Hugh_Chrisholm_1911,_p._968_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hugh_Chrisholm_1911,_p._968-74">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> The control of the high road to the Mediterranean was secured by the possession of the Hittite town of <a href="/wiki/Pitru" title="Pitru">Pitru</a><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> at the junction between the Euphrates and <a href="/wiki/Sajur" title="Sajur">Sajur</a>; thence he proceeded to conquer the <a href="/wiki/Canaan" title="Canaan">Canaanite</a>/<a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenician</a> city-states of <a href="/wiki/Byblos" title="Byblos">Byblos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tyre,_Lebanon" title="Tyre, Lebanon">Tyre</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sidon" title="Sidon">Sidon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Simyra" class="mw-redirect" title="Simyra">Simyra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Berytus" title="Berytus">Berytus</a> (<a href="/wiki/Beirut" title="Beirut">Beirut</a>), <a href="/wiki/Aradus" title="Aradus">Aradus</a> and finally <a href="/wiki/Arvad" class="mw-redirect" title="Arvad">Arvad</a> where he embarked onto a ship to sail the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean" class="mw-redirect" title="Mediterranean">Mediterranean</a>, on which he killed a <i>nahiru</i> or "sea-horse" (which <a href="/wiki/A._Leo_Oppenheim" title="A. Leo Oppenheim">A. Leo Oppenheim</a> translates as a <a href="/wiki/Narwhal" title="Narwhal">narwhal</a>) in the sea.<sup id="cite_ref-Hugh_Chrisholm_1911,_p._968_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hugh_Chrisholm_1911,_p._968-74">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> He was passionately fond of hunting and was also a great builder. The general view is that the restoration of the temple of the gods <a href="/wiki/Ashur_(god)" title="Ashur (god)">Ashur</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hadad" title="Hadad">Hadad</a> at the Assyrian capital of Assur (Ashur) was one of his initiatives.<sup id="cite_ref-Hugh_Chrisholm_1911,_p._968_74-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hugh_Chrisholm_1911,_p._968-74">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> He was succeeded by <a href="/wiki/Asharid-apal-Ekur" title="Asharid-apal-Ekur">Asharid-apal-Ekur</a> who reigned for only a short time.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ashur-bel-kala" title="Ashur-bel-kala">Ashur-bel-kala</a> (1073–1056 BC) kept the vast empire together, campaigning successfully against <a href="/wiki/Urartu" title="Urartu">Urartu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phrygia" title="Phrygia">Phrygia</a> to the north and the Arameans to the west. He maintained friendly relations with <a href="/wiki/Marduk-shapik-zeri" title="Marduk-shapik-zeri">Marduk-shapik-zeri</a> of Babylon, however upon the death of that king, he invaded Babylonia and deposed the new ruler <a href="/wiki/Kada%C5%A1man-Buria%C5%A1" title="Kadašman-Buriaš">Kadašman-Buriaš</a>, appointing <a href="/wiki/Adad-apla-iddina" title="Adad-apla-iddina">Adad-apla-iddina</a> as his vassal in Babylon. He built some of the earliest examples of both <a href="/wiki/Zoological_Gardens" class="mw-redirect" title="Zoological Gardens">Zoological Gardens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Botanical_Gardens" class="mw-redirect" title="Botanical Gardens">Botanical Gardens</a> in Ashur, collecting all manner of animals and plants from his empire, and receiving a collection of exotic animals as tributes from Egypt.
</p><p>Late in his reign, the Middle Assyrian Empire erupted into civil war, when a rebellion was orchestrated by <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tukulti-Mer&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Tukulti-Mer (page does not exist)">Tukulti-Mer</a>, a pretender to the throne of Assyria. Ashur-bel-kala eventually crushed Tukulti-Mer and his allies, however the civil war in Assyria had allowed hordes of Arameans to take advantage of the situation, and press in on Assyrian controlled territory from the west. Ashur-bel-kala counterattacked them, and conquered as far as Carchemish and the source of the <a href="/wiki/Khabur_(Tigris)" title="Khabur (Tigris)">Khabur river</a>, but by the end of his reign many of the areas of Syria and Phoenicia-Canaan to the west of these regions as far as the Mediterranean, previously under firm Assyrian control, were eventually lost to the Middle Assyrian Empire.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Society_and_law_in_the_Middle_Assyrian_Period">Society and law in the Middle Assyrian Period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Society and law in the Middle Assyrian Period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:C%2BB-Music-Fig24-AssyrianQuartet.PNG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/C%2BB-Music-Fig24-AssyrianQuartet.PNG/220px-C%2BB-Music-Fig24-AssyrianQuartet.PNG" decoding="async" width="220" height="202" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/C%2BB-Music-Fig24-AssyrianQuartet.PNG/330px-C%2BB-Music-Fig24-AssyrianQuartet.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b9/C%2BB-Music-Fig24-AssyrianQuartet.PNG 2x" data-file-width="436" data-file-height="401" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:C%2BB-Music-Fig24-AssyrianQuartet.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Assyrian <a href="/wiki/Quartet" title="Quartet">quartet</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The Middle Assyrian kingdom was well organized, and in the firm control of the king, who also functioned as the High Priest of <a href="/wiki/Anshar" title="Anshar">Ashur</a>, the state god. He had certain obligations to fulfill in the cult, and had to provide resources for the temples. The priesthood became a major power in Assyrian society. Conflicts with the priesthood are thought to have been behind the murder of king <a href="/wiki/Tukulti-Ninurta_I" title="Tukulti-Ninurta I">Tukulti-Ninurta I</a>.
</p><p>The Middle Assyrian Period was marked by the long wars fought that helped build Assyria into a <a href="/wiki/Warrior_society" class="mw-redirect" title="Warrior society">warrior society</a>. The king depended on both the citizen class and priests in his capital, and the landed nobility who supplied the horses needed by Assyria's military. Documents and letters illustrate the importance of the latter to Assyrian society. Assyria needed less artificial <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">irrigation</a> than Babylonia, and horse-breeding was extensive. Portions of elaborate texts about the care and training of them have been found. Trade was carried out in all directions. The mountain country to the north and west of Assyria was a major source of metal ore, as well as lumber. Economic factors were a common <i><a href="/wiki/Casus_belli" title="Casus belli">casus belli</a></i>.
</p><p>All free male citizens were obliged to serve in the army for a time, a system which was called the <i>ilku</i>-service. A <a href="/wiki/Legal_code" class="mw-redirect" title="Legal code">legal code</a> was produced during the 14th and 13th centuries which, among other things, clearly shows that the social position of women in Assyria was lower than that of neighbouring societies. Men were permitted to <a href="/wiki/Divorce" title="Divorce">divorce</a> their wives with no compensation paid to the latter. If a woman committed <a href="/wiki/Adultery" title="Adultery">adultery</a>, she could be beaten or put to death. It's not certain if these laws were seriously enforced, but they appear to be a backlash against some older documents that granted things like equal compensation to both partners in divorce.
</p><p>The women of the king's <a href="/wiki/Harem" title="Harem">harem</a> and their servants were also subject to harsh punishments, such as <a href="/wiki/Strike_(attack)" title="Strike (attack)">beatings</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mutilation" title="Mutilation">mutilation</a>, and death. Assyria, in general, had much harsher laws than most of the region. <a href="/wiki/Execution" class="mw-redirect" title="Execution">Executions</a> were not uncommon, nor were whippings followed by <a href="/wiki/Forced_labour" class="mw-redirect" title="Forced labour">forced labour</a>. Some offenses allowed the accused a trial under torture or duress. One tablet that covers property rights has brutal penalties for violators. A creditor could force debtors to work for him, but not sell them.
</p><p>In the Middle Assyrian Laws, <a href="/wiki/Sex_crime" class="mw-redirect" title="Sex crime">sex crimes</a> were punished identically whether they were <a href="/wiki/Homosexual" class="mw-redirect" title="Homosexual">homosexual</a> or <a href="/wiki/Heterosexual" class="mw-redirect" title="Heterosexual">heterosexual</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Nissinen_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nissinen-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> An individual faced no punishment for penetrating a cult prostitute, someone of an equal or lower <a href="/wiki/Social_class" title="Social class">social class</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Slave" class="mw-redirect" title="Slave">slaves</a>, or someone whose <a href="/wiki/Gender_role" title="Gender role">gender roles</a> were not considered solidly masculine. Such sexual relations were even seen as good <a href="/wiki/Luck" title="Luck">fortune</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> However, homosexual relationships with royal attendants, between soldiers, or with those where a social better was <a href="/wiki/Top,_bottom_and_versatile" title="Top, bottom and versatile">submissive or penetrated</a> were either treated as <a href="/wiki/Rape" title="Rape">rape</a> or seen as bad <a href="/wiki/Omens" class="mw-redirect" title="Omens">omens</a>, and punishments applied.<sup id="cite_ref-Nissinen_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nissinen-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> One historian notes that the laws would not be so detailed "if homosexual behavior were not a familiar aspect of daily life of early Mesopotamia".<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Furthermore, the article 'Homosexualität' in <i>Reallexicon der Assyriologie</i> states, "Homosexuality in itself is thus nowhere condemned as licentiousness, as immorality, as social disorder, or as transgressing any human or <a href="/wiki/Divine_law" title="Divine law">divine law</a>. Anyone could practice it freely, just as anyone could visit a <a href="/wiki/Prostitute" class="mw-redirect" title="Prostitute">prostitute</a>, provided it was done without <a href="/wiki/Sexual_violence" title="Sexual violence">violence</a> and without <a href="/wiki/Compulsive_behavior" title="Compulsive behavior">compulsion</a>, and preferably as far as taking the passive role was concerned, with specialists. That there was nothing <a href="/wiki/Sin" title="Sin">religiously amiss</a> with homosexual love <a href="/wiki/Men_who_have_sex_with_men" title="Men who have sex with men">between men</a> is seen by the fact that they prayed for divine blessing on it. It seems clear that the Mesopotamians saw nothing wrong in homosexual acts between consenting adults".<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Assyria_during_the_Bronze_Age_Collapse.2C_1200.E2.80.93936_BC"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Assyria_during_the_Bronze_Age_Collapse,_1200–936_BC">Assyria during the Bronze Age Collapse, 1200–936 BC</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Assyria during the Bronze Age Collapse, 1200–936 BC">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age_Collapse" class="mw-redirect" title="Bronze Age Collapse">Bronze Age Collapse</a> from 1200 BC to 900 BC was a <a href="/wiki/Dark_Ages_(historiography)" title="Dark Ages (historiography)">Dark Age</a> for the entire <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean" class="mw-redirect" title="Mediterranean">Mediterranean</a> and <a href="/wiki/Balkan" class="mw-redirect" title="Balkan">Balkan</a> regions, with great upheavals and mass movements of people.
</p><p>Assyria and its empire were not unduly affected by these tumultuous events for some 150 years, perhaps the only ancient power that was not, and in fact thrived for most of the period. However, upon the death of <a href="/wiki/Ashur-bel-kala" title="Ashur-bel-kala">Ashur-bel-kala</a> in 1056 BC, Assyria went into a <i>comparative</i> decline for the next 100 or so years. The empire shrank significantly, and by 1020 BC Assyria appears to have controlled only areas close to Assyria itself, essential to keeping trade routes open in eastern Aramea, South Eastern Asia Minor, central Mesopotamia and north western Iran.
</p><p>New <a href="/wiki/West_Semitic_languages" title="West Semitic languages">West Semitic-speaking</a> peoples such as the <a href="/wiki/Arameans" title="Arameans">Arameans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Suteans" title="Suteans">Suteans</a> moved into areas to the west and south of Assyria, including overrunning much of Babylonia to the south, Indo-European speaking <a href="/wiki/Iranic" class="mw-redirect" title="Iranic">Iranic</a> peoples such as the <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">Medes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Persian_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Persian people">Persians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sarmatians" title="Sarmatians">Sarmatians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Parthia" title="Parthia">Parthians</a> moved into the lands to the east of Assyria, displacing the native <a href="/wiki/Kassites" title="Kassites">Kassites</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gutians" class="mw-redirect" title="Gutians">Gutians</a> and pressuring <a href="/wiki/Elam" title="Elam">Elam</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mannea" class="mw-redirect" title="Mannea">Mannea</a> (all of which ancient non Indo-European civilisations of Ancient Iran), and to the north in <a href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a> the <a href="/wiki/Phrygians" title="Phrygians">Phrygians</a> overran that part of the Hittites not already destroyed by Assyria, and <a href="/wiki/Lydia" title="Lydia">Lydia</a> emerged, a new <a href="/wiki/Hurrian" class="mw-redirect" title="Hurrian">Hurrian</a> state named <a href="/wiki/Urartu" title="Urartu">Urartu</a> arose in the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cimmerians" title="Cimmerians">Cimmerians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colchians" class="mw-redirect" title="Colchians">Colchians</a> (Georgians) and <a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">Scythians</a> around the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>. <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> was divided and in disarray, and <a href="/wiki/Israelites" title="Israelites">Israelites</a> were battling with other <a href="/wiki/West_Asia" class="mw-redirect" title="West Asia">West Asian</a> peoples such as the <a href="/wiki/Amalekites" class="mw-redirect" title="Amalekites">Amalekites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moabites" class="mw-redirect" title="Moabites">Moabites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edomites" class="mw-redirect" title="Edomites">Edomites</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ammon" title="Ammon">Ammonites</a> and the non-Semitic-speaking <a href="/wiki/Peleset" class="mw-redirect" title="Peleset">Peleset</a>/<a href="/wiki/Philistines" title="Philistines">Philistines</a> (who have been conjectured to be one of the so-called <a href="/wiki/Sea_Peoples" title="Sea Peoples">Sea Peoples</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-AK2013_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AK2013-82">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Drews48_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Drews48-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> for the control of southern <a href="/wiki/Canaan" title="Canaan">Canaan</a>. <a href="/wiki/Dorian_Greeks" class="mw-redirect" title="Dorian Greeks">Dorian Greeks</a> usurped the earlier <a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_Greeks" class="mw-redirect" title="Mycenaean Greeks">Mycenaean Greeks</a> in western Asia Minor, and the <a href="/wiki/Sea_Peoples" title="Sea Peoples">Sea Peoples</a> ravaged the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Mediterranean" title="Eastern Mediterranean">Eastern Mediterranean</a>.
</p><p>Other new peoples, such as the <a href="/wiki/Chaldea" title="Chaldea">Chaldeans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sarmatians" title="Sarmatians">Sarmatians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arabs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nubians" title="Nubians">Nubians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kushites" class="mw-redirect" title="Kushites">Kushites</a> were to emerge later, during the <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">Neo-Assyrian Empire</a> (911-605 BC).
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:272px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Assyrian_horsemen_arabs.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Assyrian_horsemen_arabs.png/270px-Assyrian_horsemen_arabs.png" decoding="async" width="270" height="118" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Assyrian_horsemen_arabs.png/405px-Assyrian_horsemen_arabs.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Assyrian_horsemen_arabs.png/540px-Assyrian_horsemen_arabs.png 2x" data-file-width="988" data-file-height="432" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Assyrian_horsemen_arabs.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">Assyrian</a> horsemen pursue defeated <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arabs</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Despite the apparent weakness of Assyria in comparison to its former might, at heart it in fact remained a solid, well defended nation whose warriors were the best in the world<sup id="cite_ref-auto_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>. Assyria, with its stable monarchy, powerful army and secure borders was in a stronger position during this time than potential rivals such as Egypt, Babylonia, Elam, Phrygia, Urartu, Persia, Lydia and Media. Kings such as <a href="/wiki/Eriba-Adad_II" title="Eriba-Adad II">Eriba-Adad II</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ashur-rabi_II" title="Ashur-rabi II">Ashur-rabi II</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ashurnasirpal_I" title="Ashurnasirpal I">Ashurnasirpal I</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tiglath-Pileser_II" title="Tiglath-Pileser II">Tiglath-Pileser II</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ashur-Dan_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Ashur-Dan II">Ashur-Dan II</a> successfully defended Assyria's borders and upheld stability during this tumultuous time.
</p><p>Assyrian kings during this period appear to have adopted a policy of maintaining and defending a compact, secure nation and satellite colonies immediately surrounding it, and interspersed this with sporadic punitive raids and invasions of neighbouring territories when the need arose, including campaigning as far as the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean" class="mw-redirect" title="Mediterranean">Mediterranean</a> and sacking <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonia</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Neo-Assyrian_Empire">Neo-Assyrian Empire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Neo-Assyrian Empire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">Neo-Assyrian Empire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Military_history_of_the_Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Military history of the Neo-Assyrian Empire">Military history of the Neo-Assyrian Empire</a></div>

<td style="vertical-align:top; text-align:center;border:0;">
<table style="width:92%; background:transparent; text-align:center; margin:0 auto; border:0;">

<td style="border:0; padding:0; vertical-align:middle; text-align:right;"><a href="/wiki/Median_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Median Empire">Median Empire</a>

</td>
<td style="border:0; padding:0; vertical-align:middle;"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/d/d2/Blank.png" decoding="async" width="22" height="15" data-file-width="3" data-file-height="2" />

</tbody></table>
</td></tr></tbody></table></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">Neo-Assyrian Empire</a> is usually considered to have begun with the ascension of <a href="/wiki/Adad-nirari_II" title="Adad-nirari II">Adad-nirari II</a>, in 911 BC, lasting until the fall of <a href="/wiki/Nineveh" title="Nineveh">Nineveh</a> at the hands of the <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">Medes/Persians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Babylonians" class="mw-redirect" title="Babylonians">Babylonians, Chaldeans</a> in 609 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Assyria maintained a large and thriving rural population, combined with a number of well fortified cities, Major Assyrian cities during this period included; <a href="/wiki/Nineveh" title="Nineveh">Nineveh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Assur" title="Assur">Assur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kalhu" class="mw-redirect" title="Kalhu">Kalhu</a> (<a href="/wiki/Calah" class="mw-redirect" title="Calah">Calah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nimrud" title="Nimrud">Nimrud</a>), <a href="/wiki/Erbil" title="Erbil">Arbela</a> (<a href="/wiki/Erbil" title="Erbil">Erbil</a>), <a href="/wiki/Arrapha" title="Arrapha">Arrapha</a> (<a href="/wiki/Karka" title="Karka">Karka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kirkuk" title="Kirkuk">Kirkuk</a>), <a href="/wiki/Dur-Sharrukin" title="Dur-Sharrukin">Dur-Sharrukin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Imgur-Enlil" class="mw-redirect" title="Imgur-Enlil">Imgur-Enlil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carchemish" title="Carchemish">Carchemish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harran" title="Harran">Harran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tushhan" title="Tushhan">Tushhan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Til-Barsip" class="mw-redirect" title="Til-Barsip">Til-Barsip</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ekallatum" title="Ekallatum">Ekallatum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kanesh" class="mw-redirect" title="Kanesh">Kanesh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kar-Tukulti-Ninurta" title="Kar-Tukulti-Ninurta">Kar-Tukulti-Ninurta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Urhai" class="mw-redirect" title="Urhai">Urhai</a> (<a href="/wiki/Edessa" title="Edessa">Edessa</a>), <a href="/wiki/Guzana" class="mw-redirect" title="Guzana">Guzana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tell_Barri" title="Tell Barri">Kahat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amid" class="mw-redirect" title="Amid">Amid</a> (<a href="/wiki/Diyarbakir" class="mw-redirect" title="Diyarbakir">Diyarbakir</a>), <a href="/wiki/Mardin" title="Mardin">Mérida</a> (<a href="/wiki/Mardin" title="Mardin">Mardin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tabitu" class="mw-redirect" title="Tabitu">Tabitu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuhadra" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuhadra">Nuhadra</a> (<a href="/wiki/Dohuk" class="mw-redirect" title="Dohuk">Dohuk</a>), <a href="/wiki/Ivah" title="Ivah">Ivah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sepharvaim" title="Sepharvaim">Sepharvaim</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rachae" title="Rachae">Rachae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Purushanda" title="Purushanda">Purushanda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sabata_(city)" title="Sabata (city)">Sabata</a>, <a href="/wiki/Birtha_(Mesopotamia)" title="Birtha (Mesopotamia)">Birtha</a> (<a href="/wiki/Tikrit" title="Tikrit">Tikrit</a>), <a href="/wiki/Tell_Shemshara" title="Tell Shemshara">Tell Shemshara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dur-Katlimmu" class="mw-redirect" title="Dur-Katlimmu">Dur-Katlimmu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shekhna" class="mw-redirect" title="Shekhna">Shekhna</a>.
</p><p>Assyria is often noted for its brutality and cruelty during this period, although Assyrian harshness was reserved solely for those who took up arms against the Assyrian king, and none of the Assyrian kings of the Neo-Assyrian Empire or preceding Middle Assyrian Empire conducted <a href="/wiki/Genocide" title="Genocide">genocides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Massacre" title="Massacre">massacres</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_cleansing" title="Ethnic cleansing">ethnic cleansings</a> against civilian populations, non-combatant men, or women and children.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Expansion.2C_911.E2.80.93627_BC"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Expansion,_911–627_BC">Expansion, 911–627 BC</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Expansion, 911–627 BC">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Assyria once more began to expand with the rise of <a href="/wiki/Adad-nirari_II" title="Adad-nirari II">Adad-nirari II</a> in 911 BC. He cleared Aramean and other tribal peoples from Assyria's borders and began to expand in all directions into <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Iran" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Iran">Ancient Iran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a> and <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonia</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ashurnasirpal_II" title="Ashurnasirpal II">Ashurnasirpal II</a> (883–859 BC) continued this expansion apace, subjugating much of the Levant to the west, the newly arrived <a href="/wiki/Persians" title="Persians">Persians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">Medes</a> to the east, annexed central Mesopotamia from Babylon to the south, and expanded deep into <a href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a> to the north. He moved the capital from <a href="/wiki/Ashur" title="Ashur">Ashur</a> to <a href="/wiki/Kalhu" class="mw-redirect" title="Kalhu">Kalhu</a> (<a href="/wiki/Calah" class="mw-redirect" title="Calah">Calah</a>/<a href="/wiki/Nimrud" title="Nimrud">Nimrud</a>) and undertook impressive building works throughout Assyria. <a href="/wiki/Shalmaneser_III" title="Shalmaneser III">Shalmaneser III</a> (859–824 BC) projected Assyrian power even further, conquering to the foothills of the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Israel_(Samaria)" title="Kingdom of Israel (Samaria)">Israel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aram-Damascus" title="Aram-Damascus">Aram-Damascus</a>, and subjugating <a href="/wiki/Persia" class="mw-redirect" title="Persia">Persia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arabs</a> who dwelt to the south of Mesopotamia, as well as driving the <a href="/wiki/Egyptians" title="Egyptians">Egyptians</a> from <a href="/wiki/Canaan" title="Canaan">Canaan</a>. It was during the reign of Shalmaneser III that the <a href="/wiki/Arab_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Arab people">Arabs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chaldaea" class="mw-redirect" title="Chaldaea">Chaldeans</a> first enter the pages of recorded history.
</p><p>Little further expansion took place under <a href="/wiki/Shamshi-Adad_V" title="Shamshi-Adad V">Shamshi-Adad V</a> and his successor, the regent queen <a href="/wiki/Semiramis" title="Semiramis">Semiramis</a>, however when <a href="/wiki/Adad-nirari_III" title="Adad-nirari III">Adad-nirari III</a> (811–783 BC) came of age, he took the reins of power from mother and set about a relentless campaign of conquest; subjugated the Arameans, Phoenicians, Philistines, Israelites, Neo-Hittites and Edomites, Persians, Medes and Manneans, penetrating as far as the <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a>. He invaded and subjugated <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonia</a>, and then the migrant <a href="/wiki/Chaldea" title="Chaldea">Chaldean</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sutean" class="mw-redirect" title="Sutean">Sutean</a> tribes settled in south eastern Mesopotamia whom he conquered and reduced to vassalage.
</p><p>After the reign of Adad-nirari III, Assyria entered a period of instability and decline, losing its hold over most of its vassal and tributary territories by the middle of the 8th century BC, until the reign of <a href="/wiki/Tiglath-Pileser_III" title="Tiglath-Pileser III">Tiglath-Pileser III</a> (745–727 BC). He created the world's first <a href="/wiki/Professional_army" class="mw-redirect" title="Professional army">professional army</a>, introduced <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Aramaic" class="mw-redirect" title="Imperial Aramaic">Imperial Aramaic</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a> of Assyria and its vast empire, and reorganised the empire drastically. Tiglath-Pileser III conquered as far as the <a href="/wiki/East_Mediterranean" class="mw-redirect" title="East Mediterranean">East Mediterranean</a>, bringing the Greeks of Cyprus, <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Judah" title="Kingdom of Judah">Judah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philistia" title="Philistia">Philistia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samaria" title="Samaria">Samaria</a> and the whole of <a href="/wiki/Aramea" class="mw-redirect" title="Aramea">Aramea</a> under Assyrian control. Not satisfied with merely holding Babylonia in vassalage, Tiglath-Pileser deposed its king and had himself crowned king of Babylon. The imperial, economic, political, military and administrative reforms of Tiglath-Pileser III were to prove a blueprint for future empires, such as those of the Persians, Greeks, Romans, Carthaginians, Byzantines, Arabs and Turks.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Shalmaneser_V" title="Shalmaneser V">Shalmaneser V</a> reigned only briefly, but once more drove the Egyptians from southern Canaan, where they were fomenting revolt against Assyria. <a href="/wiki/Sargon_II" title="Sargon II">Sargon II</a> quickly took Samaria, effectively ending the northern <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Israel_(united_monarchy)" title="Kingdom of Israel (united monarchy)">Kingdom of Israel</a> and carrying 27,000 people away into captivity into the Israelite diaspora. He was forced to fight a war to drive out the <a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">Scythians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cimmerians" title="Cimmerians">Cimmerians</a> who had attempted to invade Assyria's vassal states of Persia and <a href="/wiki/Media_(region)" title="Media (region)">Media</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Neo-Hittite" class="mw-redirect" title="Neo-Hittite">Neo-Hittite</a> states of northern Syria were conquered, as well as <a href="/wiki/Cilicia" title="Cilicia">Cilicia</a>. <a href="/wiki/Lydia" title="Lydia">Lydia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Commagene" class="mw-redirect" title="Commagene">Commagene</a>. King <a href="/wiki/Midas" title="Midas">Midas</a> of <a href="/wiki/Phrygia" title="Phrygia">Phrygia</a>, fearful of Assyrian power, offered his hand in friendship. <a href="/wiki/Elam" title="Elam">Elam</a> was defeated and <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chaldea" title="Chaldea">Chaldea</a> reconquered. He made a new capital city named <a href="/wiki/Dur_Sharrukin" class="mw-redirect" title="Dur Sharrukin">Dur Sharrukin</a>. He was succeeded by his son <a href="/wiki/Sennacherib" title="Sennacherib">Sennacherib</a> who moved the capital to <a href="/wiki/Nineveh" title="Nineveh">Nineveh</a> and made the deported peoples work on improving Nineveh's system of irrigation canals. Nineveh was transformed into the largest city in the world at the time.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Esarhaddon" title="Esarhaddon">Esarhaddon</a> had Babylon rebuilt, he imposed a <i>vassal treaty</i> upon his Persian, Median and Parthian subjects, and he once more defeated the Scythes and Cimmerians. Tiring of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egyptian</a> interference in the Assyrian Empire, <a href="/wiki/Esarhaddon" title="Esarhaddon">Esarhaddon</a> decided to conquer Egypt. In 671 BC he crossed the <a href="/wiki/Sinai_Desert" class="mw-redirect" title="Sinai Desert">Sinai Desert</a>, invaded and took Egypt with surprising ease and speed. He drove its foreign <a href="/wiki/Nubian_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Nubian people">Nubian</a>/<a href="/wiki/Kushite" class="mw-redirect" title="Kushite">Kushite</a> and Ethiopian rulers out, destroying the <a href="/wiki/Kushite_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Kushite Empire">Kushite Empire</a> in the process. Esarhaddon declared himself "king of Egypt, Libya, and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kush" title="Kingdom of Kush">Kush</a>". Esarhaddon stationed a small army in northern Egypt and describes how; "All Ethiopians (read Nubians/Kushites) I deported from Egypt, leaving not one left to do homage to me". He installed native Egyptian princes throughout the land to rule on his behalf.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:432px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Territorial_organization_of_the_Assyrian_Empire_in_times_of_Ashurbanipal.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Territorial_organization_of_the_Assyrian_Empire_in_times_of_Ashurbanipal.svg/430px-Territorial_organization_of_the_Assyrian_Empire_in_times_of_Ashurbanipal.svg.png" decoding="async" width="430" height="394" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Territorial_organization_of_the_Assyrian_Empire_in_times_of_Ashurbanipal.svg/645px-Territorial_organization_of_the_Assyrian_Empire_in_times_of_Ashurbanipal.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Territorial_organization_of_the_Assyrian_Empire_in_times_of_Ashurbanipal.svg/860px-Territorial_organization_of_the_Assyrian_Empire_in_times_of_Ashurbanipal.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="46995" data-file-height="43029" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Territorial_organization_of_the_Assyrian_Empire_in_times_of_Ashurbanipal.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Assyrian Empire to the death of Ashurbanipal,in dark green the <i>pahitu</i>/<i>pahutu</i> (provinces),in yellow the <i>matu</i> (subjects kingdoms), in cream color the Babylon kingdom, the yellow points show other subjects kingdoms, the black points show the <i>pahitu</i>/<i>pahutu</i> (provinces) of Babylon kingdom, and the brown letters provinces that existed previously</div></div></div>
<p>Under <a href="/wiki/Ashurbanipal" title="Ashurbanipal">Ashurbanipal</a> (669–627 BC), an unusually well educated king for his time who could speak, read and write in Sumerian, Akkadian and Aramaic, Assyrian domination spanned from the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus_Mountains" title="Caucasus Mountains">Caucasus Mountains</a> (modern <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a>) in the north to <a href="/wiki/Nubia" title="Nubia">Nubia</a>, Egypt, Libya and Arabia in the south, and from the East Mediterranean, Cyprus and <a href="/wiki/Antioch" title="Antioch">Antioch</a> in the west to Persia, <a href="/wiki/Cissia_(area)" title="Cissia (area)">Cissia</a> and the Caspian Sea in the east.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Susa-destruction.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Susa-destruction.jpg/170px-Susa-destruction.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="196" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Susa-destruction.jpg/255px-Susa-destruction.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Susa-destruction.jpg/340px-Susa-destruction.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="575" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Susa-destruction.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ashurbanipal's brutal campaign against <a href="/wiki/Elam" title="Elam">Elam</a> in 647 BC is recorded in this relief.</div></div></div>
<p>Ultimately, Assyria conquered <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chaldea" title="Chaldea">Chaldea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elam" title="Elam">Elam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Median_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Median Empire">Media</a>, <a href="/wiki/Persia" class="mw-redirect" title="Persia">Persia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Urartu" title="Urartu">Urartu</a> (<a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a>), <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aramea" class="mw-redirect" title="Aramea">Aramea</a>/<a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phrygia" title="Phrygia">Phrygia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Neo-Hittites" class="mw-redirect" title="Neo-Hittites">Neo-Hittite States</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Hurrian" class="mw-redirect" title="Hurrian">Hurrian lands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arabia" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabia">Arabia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gutium" class="mw-redirect" title="Gutium">Gutium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Israel_(Samaria)" title="Kingdom of Israel (Samaria)">Israel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Judah" title="Kingdom of Judah">Judah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samarra" title="Samarra">Samarra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moab" title="Moab">Moab</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edom" title="Edom">Edom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Corduene" title="Corduene">Corduene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cilicia" title="Cilicia">Cilicia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mannea" class="mw-redirect" title="Mannea">Mannea</a>, and Cyprus, and defeated and/or exacted tribute from <a href="/wiki/Scythia" title="Scythia">Scythia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crimean_Peninsula" class="mw-redirect" title="Crimean Peninsula">Cimmeria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lydia" title="Lydia">Lydia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nubia" title="Nubia">Nubia</a>, Ethiopia and others. At its height, the Empire encompassed the whole of the modern nations of <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jordan" title="Jordan">Jordan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kuwait" title="Kuwait">Kuwait</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bahrain" title="Bahrain">Bahrain</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>, together with large swathes of <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a>.
</p>
<h4><span id="Downfall.2C_626.E2.80.93609_BC"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Downfall,_626–609_BC">Downfall, 626–609 BC</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Downfall, 626–609 BC">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Medo-Babylonian_war_against_Assyrian_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Medo-Babylonian war against Assyrian Empire">Medo-Babylonian war against Assyrian Empire</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">Assyrian Empire</a> was severely crippled following the death of <a href="/wiki/Ashurbanipal" title="Ashurbanipal">Ashurbanipal</a> in 627 BC, the nation and its empire descending into a prolonged and brutal series of civil wars involving three rival kings, <a href="/wiki/Ashur-etil-ilani" title="Ashur-etil-ilani">Ashur-etil-ilani</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sin-shumu-lishir" title="Sin-shumu-lishir">Sin-shumu-lishir</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sin-shar-ishkun" class="mw-redirect" title="Sin-shar-ishkun">Sin-shar-ishkun</a>. <a href="/wiki/Twenty-sixth_dynasty_of_Egypt" class="mw-redirect" title="Twenty-sixth dynasty of Egypt">Egypt's 26th Dynasty</a>, which had been installed by the <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">Assyrians</a> as vassals, quietly detached itself from Assyria, although it was careful to retain friendly relations.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">Scythians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cimmerians" title="Cimmerians">Cimmerians</a> took advantage of the bitter fighting among the Assyrians to raid <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">Assyrian</a> colonies, with hordes of horse-borne marauders ravaging parts of <a href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus_Mountains" title="Caucasus Mountains">Caucasus</a>, where the vassal kings of <a href="/wiki/Urartu" title="Urartu">Urartu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lydia" title="Lydia">Lydia</a> begged their <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">Assyrian</a> overlord for help in vain. They also raided the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Israel_(Samaria)" title="Kingdom of Israel (Samaria)">Israel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Judah" title="Kingdom of Judah">Judah</a> (where <a href="/wiki/Ashkelon" title="Ashkelon">Ashkelon</a> was sacked by the <a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">Scythians</a>) and all the way into <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egypt</a> whose coasts were ravaged and looted with impunity.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Iranic" class="mw-redirect" title="Iranic">Iranic</a> peoples (the <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">Medes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Persia" class="mw-redirect" title="Persia">Persians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Parthia" title="Parthia">Parthians</a>), aided by the previous <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">Assyrian</a> destruction of the hitherto dominant <a href="/wiki/Elamites" class="mw-redirect" title="Elamites">Elamites</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Iran" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Iran">Ancient Iran</a>, also took advantage of the upheavals in <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">Assyria</a> to coalesce into a powerful Median-dominated force which destroyed the <i>pre-Iranic</i> kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Mannea" class="mw-redirect" title="Mannea">Mannea</a> and absorbed the remnants of the pre-Iranic <a href="/wiki/Elamites" class="mw-redirect" title="Elamites">Elamites</a> of southern[Iran, and the equally pre-Iranic <a href="/wiki/Gutians" class="mw-redirect" title="Gutians">Gutians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manneans" class="mw-redirect" title="Manneans">Manneans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kassites" title="Kassites">Kassites</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Zagros_Mountains" title="Zagros Mountains">Zagros Mountains</a> and the Caspian Sea.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Cyaxares" title="Cyaxares">Cyaxares</a> (technically a vassal of <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">Assyria</a>), in an alliance with the <a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">Scythians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cimmerians" title="Cimmerians">Cimmerians</a>, launched a surprise attack on a civil war beleaguered Assyria in 615 BC, sacking Kalhu (the Biblical <a href="/wiki/Nimrud" title="Nimrud">Calah/Nimrud</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Arrapha" title="Battle of Arrapha">taking Arrapha</a> (modern Kirkuk) and <a href="/wiki/Gasur" class="mw-redirect" title="Gasur">Gasur</a>. <a href="/wiki/Nabopolassar" title="Nabopolassar">Nabopolassar</a>, still pinned down in southern <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> by Assyrian forces, was completely uninvolved in this major breakthrough against <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">Assyria</a>.
</p><p>Despite the sorely depleted <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">state of Assyria</a>, bitter fighting ensued; throughout 614 BC the <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">Medes</a> continued to gradually make hard fought inroads into <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">Assyria</a> itself, scoring a decisive and devastating victory over the <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian" class="mw-redirect" title="Neo-Assyrian">Assyrian</a> forces at the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Assur" title="Fall of Assur">battle of Assur</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-A_Companion_to_Assyria_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A_Companion_to_Assyria-88">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> In 613 BC, however, the Assyrians scored a number of counterattacking victories over the <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">Medes-Persians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neo-Babylonian_Empire" title="Neo-Babylonian Empire">Babylonians-Chaldeans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">Scythians</a>-<a href="/wiki/Cimmerians" title="Cimmerians">Cimmerians</a>. This led to the unification of the forces ranged against Assyria who launched a massive combined attack, finally besieging and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Nineveh_(612_BC)" title="Battle of Nineveh (612 BC)">entering Nineveh</a> in late 612 BC, with <a href="/wiki/Sinsharishkun" title="Sinsharishkun">Sin-shar-ishkun</a> being slain in the bitter street by street fighting. Despite the loss of almost all of its major cities, and in the face of overwhelming odds, Assyrian resistance continued under <a href="/wiki/Ashur-uballit_II" title="Ashur-uballit II">Ashur-uballit II</a> (612–609 BC), who fought his way out of <a href="/wiki/Nineveh" title="Nineveh">Nineveh</a> and coalesced Assyrian forces around <a href="/wiki/Harran" title="Harran">Harran</a> which finally fell in 609 BC. The same year, Ashur-uballit II besieged Harran with the help of the Egyptian army, but this failed too, and this last defeat ended the <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">Assyrian Empire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-A_Companion_to_Assyria_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A_Companion_to_Assyria-88">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> During the aftermath, Egypt, along with remnants of the <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">Assyrian</a> army, suffered a defeat at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Carchemish" title="Battle of Carchemish">battle of Carchemish</a>, in 605 BC, but the Assyrian troops did not participate to this battle as the army of the Assyrian state because certainly by 609 BC at the very latest,<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> Assyria had been destroyed as an independent political entity, although it was to launch major rebellions against the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid Empire</a> in 546 BC and 520 BC, and remained a <a href="/wiki/Geo-political" class="mw-redirect" title="Geo-political">geo-political</a> region, ethnic entity and colonised province.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Assyria_after_the_empire">Assyria after the empire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Assyria after the empire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span id="Achaemenid_Assyria.2C_Osroene.2C_As.C5.8Drist.C4.81n.2C_Athura_and_Hatra"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Achaemenid_Assyria,_Osroene,_Asōristān,_Athura_and_Hatra">Achaemenid Assyria, Osroene, Asōristān, Athura and Hatra</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Achaemenid Assyria, Osroene, Asōristān, Athura and Hatra">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Osroene" title="Osroene">Osroene</a>, <a href="/wiki/As%C5%8Drist%C4%81n" class="mw-redirect" title="Asōristān">Asōristān</a>, <a href="/wiki/Athura" class="mw-redirect" title="Athura">Athura</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hatra" title="Hatra">Hatra</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:392px;"><a href="/wiki/File:History_of_Egypt,_Chaldea,_Syria,_Babylonia_and_Assyria_(1903)_(14747728556).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/History_of_Egypt%2C_Chaldea%2C_Syria%2C_Babylonia_and_Assyria_%281903%29_%2814747728556%29.jpg/390px-History_of_Egypt%2C_Chaldea%2C_Syria%2C_Babylonia_and_Assyria_%281903%29_%2814747728556%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="390" height="131" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/History_of_Egypt%2C_Chaldea%2C_Syria%2C_Babylonia_and_Assyria_%281903%29_%2814747728556%29.jpg/585px-History_of_Egypt%2C_Chaldea%2C_Syria%2C_Babylonia_and_Assyria_%281903%29_%2814747728556%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/History_of_Egypt%2C_Chaldea%2C_Syria%2C_Babylonia_and_Assyria_%281903%29_%2814747728556%29.jpg/780px-History_of_Egypt%2C_Chaldea%2C_Syria%2C_Babylonia_and_Assyria_%281903%29_%2814747728556%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1350" data-file-height="452" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:History_of_Egypt,_Chaldea,_Syria,_Babylonia_and_Assyria_(1903)_(14747728556).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Athura in the Achaemenid period.</div></div></div>
<p>Assyria was initially ruled by the short-lived <a href="/wiki/Median_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Median Empire">Median Empire</a> (609–549 BC) after its fall. In a twist of fate, <a href="/wiki/Nabonidus" title="Nabonidus">Nabonidus</a>, the last king of <a href="/wiki/Neo-Babylonian_Empire" title="Neo-Babylonian Empire">Babylon</a> (together with his son and co-regent <a href="/wiki/Belshazzar" title="Belshazzar">Belshazzar</a>), was himself an Assyrian from <a href="/wiki/Harran" title="Harran">Harran</a>. He had overthrown the short-lived <a href="/wiki/Chaldea" title="Chaldea">Chaldean</a> dynasty in Babylonia, after which the Chaldeans disappeared from history, being fully absorbed into the native population of Babylonia. However, apart from plans to dedicate religious temples in the city of Harran, Nabonidus showed little interest in rebuilding Assyria. <a href="/wiki/Nineveh" title="Nineveh">Nineveh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kalhu" class="mw-redirect" title="Kalhu">Kalhu</a> remained in ruins with only small numbers of Assyrians living within them; conversely, a number of towns and cities, such as <a href="/wiki/Arrapkha" class="mw-redirect" title="Arrapkha">Arrapkha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guzana" class="mw-redirect" title="Guzana">Guzana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nohadra" class="mw-redirect" title="Nohadra">Nohadra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harran" title="Harran">Harran</a>, remained intact, and <a href="/wiki/Assur" title="Assur">Assur</a> and <a href="/wiki/Erbil" title="Erbil">Arbela</a> (<a href="/wiki/Irbil" class="mw-redirect" title="Irbil">Irbil</a>) were not completely destroyed, as is attested by their later revival. However, Assyria spent much of this short period in a degree of devastation, following its fall.
</p>
<h4><span id="Achaemenid_Assyria_.28549.E2.80.93330_BC.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Achaemenid_Assyria_(549–330_BC)">Achaemenid Assyria (549–330 BC)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Achaemenid Assyria (549–330 BC)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Assyria" title="Achaemenid Assyria">Achaemenid Assyria</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r923042769/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti empire</a>, 588 until 536 BC.</center></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:153px;max-width:153px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Xerxes_I_tomb_Assyrian_soldier_circa_470_BCE.jpg" decoding="async" width="151" height="291" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Xerxes_I_tomb_Assyrian_soldier_circa_470_BCE.jpg/227px-Xerxes_I_tomb_Assyrian_soldier_circa_470_BCE.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Xerxes_I_tomb_Assyrian_soldier_circa_470_BCE.jpg/302px-Xerxes_I_tomb_Assyrian_soldier_circa_470_BCE.jpg 2x" data-file-width="422" data-file-height="814" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><center>Assyrian soldier in the Achaemenid army circa 470 BC, <a href="/wiki/Xerxes_I" title="Xerxes I">Xerxes I</a> tomb .</center></div></div></div></div></div>
<p>After the <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">Medes</a> were overthrown by the <a href="/wiki/Persian_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Persian people">Persians</a> as the dominant force in Ancient Iran, Assyria was ruled by the <a href="/wiki/Persia" class="mw-redirect" title="Persia">Persian</a> <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid Empire</a> (as <a href="/wiki/Athura" class="mw-redirect" title="Athura">Athura</a>) from 549 BC to 330 BC (see <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Assyria" title="Achaemenid Assyria">Achaemenid Assyria</a>). Between 546 and 545 BC, Assyria rebelled against the new Persian Dynasty, which had usurped the previous Median dynasty. The rebellion centered around <a href="/wiki/Tyareh" class="mw-redirect" title="Tyareh">Tyareh</a> was eventually quashed by <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">Cyrus the Great</a>.
</p><p>Assyria seems to have recovered dramatically, and flourished during this period. It became a major agricultural and administrative centre of the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid Empire</a>, and its soldiers were a mainstay of the Persian Army.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> In fact, Assyria even became powerful enough to raise another full-scale revolt against the Persian empire in 520–519 BC.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Persian_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Persian people">Persians</a> had spent centuries under Assyrian domination (their first ruler <a href="/wiki/Achaemenes" title="Achaemenes">Achaemenes</a> and his successors, having been vassals of Assyria), and Assyrian influence can be seen in Achaemenid art, infrastructure and administration. Early Persian rulers saw themselves as successors to <a href="/wiki/Ashurbanipal" title="Ashurbanipal">Ashurbanipal</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamian" class="mw-redirect" title="Mesopotamian">Mesopotamian</a> <a href="/wiki/Aramaic" title="Aramaic">Aramaic</a> was retained as the <i>lingua franca</i> of the empire for over two hundred years, and Greek writers such as <a href="/wiki/Thucydides" title="Thucydides">Thucydides</a> still referred to it as <i>the Assyrian language</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nineveh" title="Nineveh">Nineveh</a> was never rebuilt however, and 200 years after it was sacked <a href="/wiki/Xenophon" title="Xenophon">Xenophon</a> reported only small numbers of Assyrians living amongst its ruins. Conversely the ancient city of <a href="/wiki/Assur" title="Assur">Assur</a> once more became a rich and prosperous entity.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It was in 5th century BC Assyria that the <a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">Syriac language</a> and <a href="/wiki/Syriac_script" class="mw-redirect" title="Syriac script">Syriac script</a> evolved. Five centuries later these were later to have a global influence as the liturgical language and written script for <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Christianity" title="Syriac Christianity">Syriac Christianity</a> and its accompanying <a href="/wiki/Syriac_literature" title="Syriac literature">Syriac literature</a> which also emerged in Assyria before spreading throughout the <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>, The <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Subcontinent" class="mw-redirect" title="Indian Subcontinent">Indian Subcontinent</a> and <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Macedonian_and_Seleucid_Assyria">Macedonian and Seleucid Assyria</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Macedonian and Seleucid Assyria">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 332 BC, Assyria fell to <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(ancient_kingdom)" title="Macedonia (ancient kingdom)">Macedonian</a> Emperor, who called the inhabitants <i>Assyrioi</i>. The <a href="/wiki/Macedonian_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Macedonian Empire">Macedonian Empire</a> (332–312) was partitioned in 312 BC. It thereafter became part of the <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid Empire</a> (312 BC). It is from this period that the later <a href="/wiki/Name_of_Syria" title="Name of Syria"><i>Syria</i> vs <i>Assyria</i> naming controversy arises</a>, the Seleucids applied the name 'Syria' which is a 9th-century BC <a href="/wiki/Indo-Anatolian" class="mw-redirect" title="Indo-Anatolian">Indo-Anatolian</a> derivation of 'Assyria' (see <a href="/wiki/Etymology_of_Syria" class="mw-redirect" title="Etymology of Syria">Etymology of Syria</a>) not only to Assyria itself, but also to the Levantine lands to the west (historically known as <a href="/wiki/Aram_(biblical_region)" class="mw-redirect" title="Aram (biblical region)">Aram</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eber_Nari" class="mw-redirect" title="Eber Nari">Eber Nari</a>), which had been part of the Assyrian empire but, the north east corner aside, never a part of Assyria proper.
</p><p>When the Seleucids lost control of Assyria proper, the name <i>Syria</i> survived but was erroneously applied not only to the land of Assyria itself, but also now to <a href="/wiki/Aramea" class="mw-redirect" title="Aramea">Aramea</a> (also known as <a href="/wiki/Eber_Nari" class="mw-redirect" title="Eber Nari">Eber Nari</a>) to the west that had once been part of the Assyrian empire, but apart from the north eastern corner, had never been a part of Assyria itself, nor inhabited by Assyrians. This was to lead to both the Assyrians from Northern Mesopotamia and the Arameans and Phoenicians from the Levant being collectively dubbed <i>Syrians</i> (and later also <i>Syriacs</i>) in <a href="/wiki/Greco-Roman_world" title="Greco-Roman world">Greco-Roman</a> and later European culture, regardless of ethnicity, history or geography.
</p><p>During Seleucid rule, Assyrians ceased to hold the senior military, economic and civil positions they had enjoyed under the Achaemenids, being largely replaced by Greeks. The Greek language also replaced Mesopotamian East Aramaic as the lingua franca of the empire, although this did not affect the Assyrian population themselves, who were not Hellenised during the Seleucid era.
</p><p>During the Seleucid period in southern Mesopotamia, <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a> was gradually abandoned in favour of a new city named <a href="/wiki/Seleucia_on_the_Tigris" class="mw-redirect" title="Seleucia on the Tigris">Seleucia on the Tigris</a>, effectively bringing an end to <i>Babylonia</i> as a geo-political entity.
</p>
<h4><span id="Parthian_Assyria_.28150_BC_.E2.80.93_225_AD.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Parthian_Assyria_(150_BC_–_225_AD)">Parthian Assyria (150 BC – 225 AD)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Parthian Assyria (150 BC – 225 AD)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Adiabene" title="Adiabene">Adiabene</a></div>
<p>By 150 BC, Assyria was largely under the control of the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthian Empire</a>. The Parthians seem to have exercised only loose control over Assyria, and between the mid 2nd century BC and 4th century AD a number of <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian" class="mw-redirect" title="Neo-Assyrian">Neo-Assyrian</a> states arose; these included the ancient capital of <a href="/wiki/Assur" title="Assur">Assur</a> itself, <a href="/wiki/Adiabene" title="Adiabene">Adiabene</a> with its capital of <a href="/wiki/Irbid" title="Irbid">Arbela</a> (modern <a href="/wiki/Irbil" class="mw-redirect" title="Irbil">Irbil</a>), <a href="/wiki/Beth_Nuhadra" class="mw-redirect" title="Beth Nuhadra">Beth Nuhadra</a> with its capital of <a href="/wiki/Nohadra" class="mw-redirect" title="Nohadra">Nohadra</a> (modern <a href="/wiki/Dohuk" class="mw-redirect" title="Dohuk">Dohuk</a>), <a href="/wiki/Osroene" title="Osroene">Osroene</a>, with its capitals of <a href="/wiki/Edessa" title="Edessa">Edessa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amid" class="mw-redirect" title="Amid">Amid</a> (modern <a href="/wiki/Sanliurfa" class="mw-redirect" title="Sanliurfa">Sanliurfa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diyarbakir" class="mw-redirect" title="Diyarbakir">Diyarbakir</a>), <a href="/wiki/Hatra" title="Hatra">Hatra</a>, and "ܒܝܬܓܪܡܝ" (<a href="/wiki/Beth_Garmai" title="Beth Garmai">Beth Garmai</a>) with its capital at <a href="/wiki/Arrapha" title="Arrapha">Arrapha</a> (modern <a href="/wiki/Kirkuk" title="Kirkuk">Kirkuk</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Adiabenian rulers converted to <a href="/wiki/Second_Temple_Judaism" title="Second Temple Judaism">Judaism</a> from <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Mesopotamian_religion" title="Ancient Mesopotamian religion">paganism</a> in the 1st century.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> After 115 AD, there are no historic traces of Jewish royalty in Adiabene.
