</p><p>These freedoms were accompanied by a major Assyrian cultural revival, and temples to the Assyrian national gods <a href="/wiki/Ashur" title="Ashur">Ashur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sin" title="Sin">Sin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hadad" title="Hadad">Hadad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ishtar" class="mw-redirect" title="Ishtar">Ishtar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ninurta" title="Ninurta">Ninurta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tammuz_(deity)" class="mw-redirect" title="Tammuz (deity)">Tammuz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shamash" class="mw-redirect" title="Shamash">Shamash</a> were once more dedicated throughout Assyria and Upper Mesopotamia during this period.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition, <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> arrived in Assyria soon after the death of Christ and the Assyrians began to gradually convert to Christianity from the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Mesopotamian_religion" title="Ancient Mesopotamian religion">ancient Mesopotamian religion</a> during the period between the early first and third centuries. Assyria became an important centre of <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Christianity" title="Syriac Christianity">Syriac Christianity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Literature" class="mw-redirect" title="Syriac Literature">Syriac Literature</a>, with the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_East" title="Church of the East">Church of the East</a> evolving in Assyria, and the <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Orthodox_Church" title="Syriac Orthodox Church">Syriac Orthodox Church</a> partly also, with Osroene becoming the first independent Christian state in history.<sup id="cite_ref-Winkler_p._1_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Winkler_p._1-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Roman_Assyria_.28116.E2.80.938.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Roman_Assyria_(116–8)">Roman Assyria (116–8)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Roman Assyria (116–8)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Assyria_(Roman_province)" title="Assyria (Roman province)">Assyria (Roman province)</a></div>
<p>However, in 116, under <a href="/wiki/Trajan" title="Trajan">Trajan</a>, Assyria and its independent states were briefly taken over by Rome as the <a href="/wiki/Assyria_(Roman_province)" title="Assyria (Roman province)">province of Assyria</a>. The Assyrian kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Adiabene" title="Adiabene">Adiabene</a> was destroyed as an independent state during this period. Roman rule lasted only a few years, and the Parthians once more regained control with the help of the Assyrians, who were incited to overthrow the Roman garrisons by the Parthian king. However, a number of Assyrians were conscripted into the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Army" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Army">Roman Army</a>, with many serving in the region of <a href="/wiki/Hadrian%27s_Wall" title="Hadrian&#39;s Wall">Hadrian's Wall</a> in <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Roman Britain</a>, and inscriptions in Aramaic made by soldiers have been discovered in <a href="/wiki/Northern_England" title="Northern England">Northern England</a> dating from the second century.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>With loose Parthian rule restored, Assyria and its patchwork of states continued much as they had before the Roman interregnum, although Assyria and Mesopotamia as a whole became a front line between the Roman and Parthian empires. Other new religious movements also emerged in the form of <a href="/wiki/Gnosticism" title="Gnosticism">gnostic sects</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Mandeanism" class="mw-redirect" title="Mandeanism">Mandeanism</a>, as well as the now extinct <a href="/wiki/Manichean" class="mw-redirect" title="Manichean">Manichean</a> religion.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Christian_period">Christian period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Christian period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span id="Sassanid_Assyria_.28226_.E2.80.93_c._650.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Sassanid_Assyria_(226_–_c._650)">Sassanid Assyria (226 – c. 650)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Sassanid Assyria (226 – c. 650)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/As%C5%8Drist%C4%81n" class="mw-redirect" title="Asōristān">Asōristān</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:362px;"><a href="/wiki/File:N-Mesopotamia_and_Syria.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/N-Mesopotamia_and_Syria.svg/360px-N-Mesopotamia_and_Syria.svg.png" decoding="async" width="360" height="207" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/N-Mesopotamia_and_Syria.svg/540px-N-Mesopotamia_and_Syria.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/N-Mesopotamia_and_Syria.svg/720px-N-Mesopotamia_and_Syria.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="2185" data-file-height="1254" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:N-Mesopotamia_and_Syria.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Upper_Mesopotamia" title="Upper Mesopotamia">Upper Mesopotamia</a> and Syria in the <a href="/wiki/Early_Christian" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Christian">early Christian</a> period, with <a href="/wiki/Edessa" title="Edessa">Edessa</a> in the left upper quadrant</div></div></div>
<p>In 226, Assyria was largely taken over by the <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sasanian Empire</a>. After driving out the Romans and Parthians, the Sassanid rulers set about <a href="/wiki/Annexing" class="mw-redirect" title="Annexing">annexing</a> the independent states within Assyria during the mid- to late 3rd century, the last being Assur itself in the late 250's to early 260's. Christianity continued to spread, and many of the ethnically Assyrian churches that exist today are among the oldest in the world. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Orthodox_Church" title="Syriac Orthodox Church">Syriac Orthodox Church</a> is purported to have been founded by <a href="/wiki/St_Peter" class="mw-redirect" title="St Peter">St Peter</a> himself in 67 AD.
</p><p>Nevertheless, although predominantly Christian, a minority of Assyrians still held onto their ancient <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamian_religion" class="mw-redirect" title="Mesopotamian religion">Mesopotamian religion</a> until as late as the 10th or 11th century AD.<sup id="cite_ref-nineveh.com_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nineveh.com-100">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> The Assyrians lived in a province known as <a href="/wiki/Asuristan" class="mw-redirect" title="Asuristan">Asuristan</a>, and the region was on the frontier of the Byzantine and Sassanian empires.
</p><p>The land was known as <a href="/wiki/As%C5%8Drist%C4%81n" class="mw-redirect" title="Asōristān">Asōristān</a> (the Sassanid Persian name meaning "Land of the Assyrians") during this period, and became the birthplace of the distinct <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_East" title="Church of the East">Church of the East</a> (now split into the <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Church_of_the_East" title="Assyrian Church of the East">Assyrian Church of the East</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Church_of_the_East" title="Ancient Church of the East">Ancient Church of the East</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chaldean_Catholic_Church" title="Chaldean Catholic Church">Chaldean Catholic Church</a>) and a centre of the <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Orthodox_Church" title="Syriac Orthodox Church">Syriac Orthodox Church</a>, with a flourishing <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Christians" class="mw-redirect" title="Syriac Christians">Syriac</a> (<a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">Assyrian</a>) Christian culture which exists there to this day. Temples were still being dedicated to the national god <a href="/wiki/Ashur_(god)" title="Ashur (god)">Ashur</a> (as well as other Mesopotamian gods) in his home city, in <a href="/wiki/Harran" title="Harran">Harran</a> and elsewhere during the 4th and 5th centuries AD, indicating the ancient pre-Christian Assyrian identity was still extant to some degree.
</p><p>During the Sasanian period, much of what had once been <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonia</a> in southern Mesopotamia was incorporated into Assyria, and in effect the whole of Mesopotamia came to be known as Asōristān. Parts of Assyria appear to have been semi independent as late as the latter part of the 4th century AD, with a king named <a href="/wiki/Sennacherib_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Sennacherib II">Sennacherib II</a> reputedly ruling the northern reaches in 370s AD.
</p>
<h4><span id="Arab_Islamic_conquest_.28630.E2.80.93780.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Arab_Islamic_conquest_(630–780)">Arab Islamic conquest (630–780)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Arab Islamic conquest (630–780)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Centuries of constant warfare between the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sassanid_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Sassanid Empire">Sassanid Empire</a> left both empires exhausted, which made both of them open to loss in a war against the <a href="/wiki/Muslim" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> Arab army, under the newfound <a href="/wiki/Rashidun_Caliphate" title="Rashidun Caliphate">Rashidun Caliphate</a>. After the early Islamic conquests, Assyria was dissolved as an official administrative entity by an empire. Under Arab rule, Mesopotamia as a whole underwent a gradual process of further <a href="/wiki/Arabisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabisation">Arabisation</a> and the beginning of <a href="/wiki/Islamification" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamification">Islamification</a>, and the region saw a large influx of <i>non indigenous</i> Arabs, <a href="/wiki/Kurds" title="Kurds">Kurds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iranian_peoples" title="Iranian peoples">Iranian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Turkic_peoples" title="Turkic peoples">Turkic peoples</a>.
</p><p>However, the indigenous <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">Assyrian</a> population of northern Mesopotamia retained their language, religion, culture and identity.
</p><p>Under the Arab Islamic empires, the Christian Assyrians were classed as <i><a href="/wiki/Dhimmis" class="mw-redirect" title="Dhimmis">dhimmis</a></i>, who had certain restrictions imposed upon them. Assyrians were thus excluded from specific duties and occupations reserved for Muslims, they did not enjoy the same political rights as Muslims, their word was not equal to that of a Muslim in legal and civil matters without a Muslim witness, they were subject to payment of a special tax (<a href="/wiki/Jizyah" class="mw-redirect" title="Jizyah">jizyah</a>) and they were banned from spreading their religion further in Muslim-ruled lands. However, personal matters such as marriage and divorce were governed by the cultural laws of the Assyrians.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For those reasons, and even during the Sassanian period before Islamic rule, The <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Church_of_the_East" title="Assyrian Church of the East">Assyrian Church of the East</a> formed a church structure that spread <a href="/wiki/East_Syrian_Rite" class="mw-redirect" title="East Syrian Rite">Nestorian Christianity</a> to as far away as China, in order to proselytize away from Muslim-ruled regions In Iran and their homeland in Mesopotamia, with evidence of their massive church structure being the <a href="/wiki/Nestorian_Stele" class="mw-redirect" title="Nestorian Stele">Nestorian Stele</a>, an artifact found in China which documented over 100 years of Christian history in China from 600 to 781 AD.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Assyrian Christians maintained relations with fellow Christians in <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a> throughout the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>. In the 12th century AD, Assyrian priests interceded on behalf of persecuted Arab Muslims in Georgia.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>.
</p>
<h4><span id="Mongol_Empire_.281200.E2.80.931300.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Mongol_Empire_(1200–1300)">Mongol Empire (1200–1300)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Mongol Empire (1200–1300)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The first signs of trouble for the Assyrians started in the 13th century, when the Mongols first <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire#Middle_East" title="Mongol Empire">invaded</a> the Near East after the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Baghdad_(1258)" title="Siege of Baghdad (1258)">fall of Baghdad in 1258</a> to <a href="/wiki/Hulagu_Khan" title="Hulagu Khan">Hulagu Khan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> Assyrians at first did very well under Mongol rule, as the <a href="/wiki/Shamanist" class="mw-redirect" title="Shamanist">Shamanist</a> Mongols were sympathetic to them, with Assyrian priests having traveled to Mongolia centuries before. The Mongols in fact spent most of their time oppressing Muslims and Jews, outlawing the practice of circumcision and <a href="/wiki/Halal#Dhabihah:_method_of_slaughter" title="Halal">halal butchery</a>, as they found them repulsive and violent.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Therefore, as one of the only groups in the region looked at in a good light, Assyrians were given special privileges and powers, with Hülegü even appointing an <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Christian" class="mw-redirect" title="Assyrian Christian">Assyrian Christian</a> governor to <a href="/wiki/Erbil" title="Erbil">Erbil</a> (<a href="/wiki/Erbil" title="Erbil">Arbela</a>), and allowing the <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Orthodox_Church" title="Syriac Orthodox Church">Syriac Orthodox Church</a> to build a church there.<sup id="cite_ref-novacek261_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-novacek261-108">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Syriac_Christianity.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Syriac_Christianity.svg/220px-Syriac_Christianity.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="118" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Syriac_Christianity.svg/330px-Syriac_Christianity.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Syriac_Christianity.svg/440px-Syriac_Christianity.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1273" data-file-height="685" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Syriac_Christianity.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aramaic language and <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Christianity" title="Syriac Christianity">Syriac Christianity</a> in the Middle East and Central Asia until being largely annihilated by <a href="/wiki/Tamerlane" class="mw-redirect" title="Tamerlane">Tamerlane</a> in the 14th century</div></div></div>
<p>However, the Mongol rulers in the Near East eventually converted to <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>. Sustained persecutions of Christians throughout the entirety of the <a href="/wiki/Ilkhanate" title="Ilkhanate">Ilkhanate</a> began in earnest in 1295 under the rule of <a href="/wiki/O%C3%AFrat" class="mw-redirect" title="Oïrat">Oïrat</a> amir <a href="/wiki/Nawr%C5%ABz_(Mongol_emir)" title="Nawrūz (Mongol emir)">Nauruz</a>, which affected the indigenous Christians greatly.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> During the reign of the Ilkhan <a href="/wiki/%C3%96ljeit%C3%BC" class="mw-redirect" title="Öljeitü">Öljeitü</a>, the  inhabitants of Erbil seized control of the citadel and much of the city in rebellion against the Muslims. In spring 1310, the Mongol <a href="/wiki/Malik" title="Malik">Malik</a> (governor) of the region attempted to seize it from them with the help of the <a href="/wiki/Kurds" title="Kurds">Kurds</a> and Arabs, but was defeated. After his defeat he decided to siege the city. The Assyrians held out for three months, but the citadel was at last taken by Ilkhanate troops and Arab, Turkic and Kurdish tribesmen on July 1, 1310. The defenders of the citadel fought to the last man, and many of the inhabitants of the lower town were subsequently massacred.
</p><p>Regardless of these hardships, the Assyrian people remained numerically dominant in the north of Mesopotamia as late as the 14th century AD, and the city of <a href="/wiki/Assur" title="Assur">Assur</a> functioned as their religious and cultural capital.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The seat of the <a href="/wiki/Catholicos" title="Catholicos">Catholicos</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_East" title="Church of the East">Church of the East</a> was <a href="/wiki/Seleucia-Ctesiphon" class="mw-redirect" title="Seleucia-Ctesiphon">Seleucia-Ctesiphon</a>, not Assur. In the mid-14th century the <a href="/wiki/Muslim" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> Turkish ruler <a href="/wiki/Tamurlane" class="mw-redirect" title="Tamurlane">Tamurlane</a> conducted a religiously motivated massacre of the indigenous Christians, and entirely destroyed the vast Church of the East structure established throughout the <a href="/wiki/Far_East" title="Far East">Far East</a> outside what had been the <a href="/wiki/Sasanid_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Sasanid Empire">Sasanid Empire</a>, with the exception of the <a href="/wiki/St_Thomas_Christians" class="mw-redirect" title="St Thomas Christians">St Thomas Christians</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Malabar_Coast" title="Malabar Coast">Malabar Coast</a> in India, who numbered 4.2 million in the 2011 census of Kerala.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> After Timur's campaign, ancient Assyria's cultural and religious capital of <a href="/wiki/Assur" title="Assur">Assur</a> fell entirely into ruins and part of it was used as a graveyard until the 1970s.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Breakup_of_the_Church_of_the_East_.281552.E2.80.931830.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Breakup_of_the_Church_of_the_East_(1552–1830)">Breakup of the Church of the East (1552–1830)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Breakup of the Church of the East (1552–1830)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Around 100 years after the massacres by Timur, a religious schism known as the <a href="/wiki/Schism_of_1552" title="Schism of 1552">Schism of 1552</a> occurred among the Christians of northern Mesopotamia. A large number of followers of the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_East" title="Church of the East">Church of the East</a> were dissatisfied with the leadership of the Church, at this point based in the <a href="/wiki/Rabban_Hormizd_Monastery" title="Rabban Hormizd Monastery">Rabban Hormizd Monastery</a> near <a href="/wiki/Alqosh" title="Alqosh">Alqosh</a>, and in particular with the system of hereditary succession of the patriarch. Three bishops elected the abbot of the monastery, <a href="/wiki/Shimun_VIII_Yohannan_Sulaqa" title="Shimun VIII Yohannan Sulaqa">Shimun VIII Yohannan Sulaqa</a>, as a rival patriarch. These did not have the rank of <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_bishop" title="Metropolitan bishop">metropolitan bishop</a>, which was required for appointing a patriarch and which was granted only to members of the patriarch's family. Sulaqa therefore went to Rome to be made a patriarch, entered into communion with the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> and was appointed "Patriarch of <a href="/wiki/Mosul" title="Mosul">Mosul</a> in Eastern Syria"<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> or "Patriarch of the Chaldean church of Mosul"<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Julius_III" title="Pope Julius III">Pope Julius III</a> in 1553. He won support only in <a href="/wiki/Diyarbak%C4%B1r" title="Diyarbakır">Diyarbakır</a> (known also as <a href="/wiki/Amid" class="mw-redirect" title="Amid">Amid</a>), where he set up his residence, and in <a href="/wiki/Mardin" title="Mardin">Mardin</a>. In 1555, he was killed by the Turkish authorities after being denounced by the traditionalist patriarch, but the metropolitans he had ordained elected a successor for him, initiating the Shimun line of patriarchs, all of whom took the name Shimun (Simon). The patriarchs of this line requested and obtained confirmation from Rome only until 1583. In 1672 they clearly broke off communion with Rome, but continued as a line of patriarchs independent from that at Alqosh, with their seat, from then on, at <a href="/wiki/Qodchanis" class="mw-redirect" title="Qodchanis">Qodchanis</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Hakkari" title="Hakkari">Hakkari</a> mountains.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> In a letter of 29 June 1653, 19 years before the Shimun line broke off relations with Rome, <a href="/wiki/Shimun_XI_Eshuyow" title="Shimun XI Eshuyow">Shimun XI Eshuyow</a> (1638–1656) called himself Patriarch of the Chaldeans. There is no record of a response from Rome confirming him as Catholic patriarch.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Biblical_Aramaic" title="Biblical Aramaic">Biblical Aramaic</a> was until recently called Chaldaic or Chaldee,<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> and East Syrian Christians, whose <a href="/wiki/Liturgy" title="Liturgy">liturgical language</a> was and is a form of <a href="/wiki/Aramaic" title="Aramaic">Aramaic</a>, were called Chaldeans,<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> as an ethnic, not a religious term. <a href="/wiki/Hormuzd_Rassam" title="Hormuzd Rassam">Hormuzd Rassam</a> (1826–1910) still applied the term "Chaldeans" no less to those not in communion with Rome than to the Catholic Chaldeans<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> and stated that "the present Chaldeans, with a few exceptions, speak the same dialect used in the <a href="/wiki/Targum" title="Targum">Targum</a>, and in some parts of <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Ezra" title="Book of Ezra">Ezra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Daniel" title="Book of Daniel">Daniel</a>, which are called 'Chaldee'."<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Long before 1672, the Shimun line, as it "gradually returned to the traditional worship of the Church of the East, thereby losing the allegiance of the western regions",<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> moved from Turkish-controlled Diyarbakır to <a href="/wiki/Urmia" title="Urmia">Urmia</a> in Persia. The bishopric of Diyarbakır became subject to the Alqosh patriarch. Bishop <a href="/wiki/Joseph_I_(Chaldean_Patriarch)" title="Joseph I (Chaldean Patriarch)">Joseph</a> of Diyarbakır converted to the Catholic faith in 1667 or 1668. In 1677, he obtained recognition from the Turkish authorities as invested with independent power in Diyarbakır and Mardin, and in 1681 he was recognized by Rome as "patriarch of the Chaldean nation deprived of its patriarch". Thus was instituted the Josephite line, a third line of patriarchs.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the Alqosh line, Eliya VII (1591–1617), Eliya VIII (1617–1660) and Eliya IX (1660–1700) contacted Rome at various times but without establishing union.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> Union was achieved in 1771 under Eliya XI, who died in 1778. His successor Eliya XII, after sending his profession of faith to Rome and receiving confirmation as Catholic patriarch, adopted a traditionalist position in 1779. His opponents elected <a href="/wiki/Yohannan_VIII_Hormizd" title="Yohannan VIII Hormizd">Yohannan Hormizd</a>, a young nephew of Eliya XI, whom Eliya XI had intended to be his successor. Although Yohannan Hormizd won the support of most of the followers of the Alqosh patriarchate, Rome considered his election to be irregular and, instead of accepting him as patriarch, merely confirmed him as metropolitan of Mosul and patriarchal administrator. He was thus granted the powers and the insignia of a patriarch, but not the title. It made the same arrangement in Diyarbakır, appointing as patriarchal administrator <a href="/wiki/Joseph_V_Augustine_Hindi" title="Joseph V Augustine Hindi">Augustine Hindi</a>, a nephew of <a href="/wiki/Joseph_IV_(Chaldean_Patriarch)" title="Joseph IV (Chaldean Patriarch)">Joseph IV</a>, whom his uncle wished to be his successor as patriarch. There were thus two traditionalist patriarchates (the Eliya line and the Shimun) and, under administrators, two Catholic patriarchates (Diyarbakır and Alqosh/Mosul).
</p><p>In 1804, Eliya XI died and had no traditionalist successor. Augustine Hindi died in 1827 and, in 1830, Rome appointed Yohannan Hormizd as <a href="/wiki/Patriarch_of_Babylon" class="mw-redirect" title="Patriarch of Babylon">patriarch of all the Catholics</a>. The Shimun line, which had been the first to enter union with Rome, remained at the head of the traditionalist church that in 1976 adopted the name <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Church_of_the_East" title="Assyrian Church of the East">Assyrian Church of the East</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> and that continued to be in the hands of the same family until the death in 1975 of <a href="/wiki/Shimun_XXI_Eshai" title="Shimun XXI Eshai">Shimun XXI Eshai</a>. At the same time, the originally traditionalist Alqosh line continues, without hereditary succession, at the head of the <a href="/wiki/Chaldean_Catholic_Church" title="Chaldean Catholic Church">Chaldean Catholic Church</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_history">Modern history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Modern history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span id="Ottoman_Empire_.281900.E2.80.931928.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Ottoman_Empire_(1900–1928)">Ottoman Empire (1900–1928)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Ottoman Empire (1900–1928)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Burning_of_Assyrians.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Burning_of_Assyrians.jpg/220px-Burning_of_Assyrians.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="130" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Burning_of_Assyrians.jpg/330px-Burning_of_Assyrians.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Burning_of_Assyrians.jpg/440px-Burning_of_Assyrians.jpg 2x" data-file-width="540" data-file-height="319" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Burning_of_Assyrians.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The burning of bodies of Christian women by Kurdish women, to recover the gold and precious stones they were supposed to have swallowed during the Assyrian Genocide<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>After these splits, the Assyrians suffered a number of religiously and ethnically motivated massacres throughout the 17th, 18th and 19th centuries,<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> such as the <a href="/wiki/Massacres_of_Badr_Khan" class="mw-redirect" title="Massacres of Badr Khan">Massacres of Badr Khan</a> which resulted in the massacre of over 10,000 Assyrians in the 1840s,<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> culminating in the large scale <a href="/wiki/Hamidian_massacres" title="Hamidian massacres">Hamidian massacres</a> of unarmed men, women and children by <a href="/wiki/Turkish_people" title="Turkish people">Turks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kurds" title="Kurds">Kurds</a> in the 1890s at the hands of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> and its associated (largely <a href="/wiki/Kurds" title="Kurds">Kurdish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arab" class="mw-redirect" title="Arab">Arab</a>) militias, which greatly reduced their numbers, particularly in southeastern Turkey where over 25,000 Assyrians were murdered.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Adana_massacre" class="mw-redirect" title="Adana massacre">Adana massacre</a> of 1909 largely aimed at Armenian Christians also accounted for the murder of some 1,500 Assyrians.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Assyrians suffered a further catastrophic series of events during <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> in the form of the religiously and ethnically motivated <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Genocide" class="mw-redirect" title="Assyrian Genocide">Assyrian Genocide</a> at the hands of the <a href="/wiki/Ottomans" class="mw-redirect" title="Ottomans">Ottomans</a> and their <a href="/wiki/Kurdish_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Kurdish people">Kurdish</a> and Arab allies from 1915 to 1918.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> Some sources claim that the highest number of Assyrians killed during the period was 750,000, while a 1922 Assyrian assessment set it at 275,000. The Assyrian Genocide ran largely in conjunction with the similarly ethno-religiously motivated <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Genocide" title="Armenian Genocide">Armenian Genocide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greek_Genocide" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek Genocide">Greek Genocide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Great_Famine_of_Mount_Lebanon" title="Great Famine of Mount Lebanon">Great Famine of Mount Lebanon</a>.
</p><p>In reaction against Ottoman cruelty, the Assyrians took up arms, and an <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_war_of_independence" class="mw-redirect" title="Assyrian war of independence">Assyrian war of independence</a> was fought during <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> which took place in what is today south eastern Turkey, northern Iraq, north western Iran and north eastern Syria. For a time, the Assyrians fought successfully against overwhelming numbers, scoring a number of victories against the Ottomans and Kurds, and also hostile Arab and Iranian groups. However, due to the collapse of the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian Empire</a>—due to the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Revolution" title="Russian Revolution">Russian Revolution</a>—and the similar collapse of the <a href="/wiki/Occupation_of_Turkish_Armenia" class="mw-redirect" title="Occupation of Turkish Armenia">Armenian Defense</a>, the Assyrians were left without allies. As a result, the Assyrians were vastly outnumbered, outgunned, surrounded, cut off, and without supplies. The only option they had was to flee the region into northwest Iran and fight their way, with around 50,000 civilians in tow, to British train lines going to <a href="/wiki/Mandatory_Iraq" title="Mandatory Iraq">Mandatory Iraq</a>. The sizable Assyrian presence in south eastern Anatolia which had endured for over four millennia was thus reduced to no more than 15,000 by the end of World War I, and by 1924 many of those who remained were forcibly expelled in a display of <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_cleansing" title="Ethnic cleansing">ethnic cleansing</a> by the Turkish government, with many leaving and later founding villages in the <a href="/wiki/Sapna_valley" title="Sapna valley">Sapna</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nahla_valley" class="mw-redirect" title="Nahla valley">Nahla valleys</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Dohuk_Governorate" title="Dohuk Governorate">Dohuk Governorate</a> of Iraq.
</p><p>In 1920 the Assyrian settlements in <a href="/wiki/Mindan" title="Mindan">Mindan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baquba" class="mw-redirect" title="Baquba">Baquba</a> were attacked by <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Arabs" class="mw-redirect" title="Iraqi Arabs">Iraqi Arabs</a>, but the Assyrian tribesmen displayed their military prowess by successfully defeating and driving off the Arab forces.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-136">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> The Assyrians also sided with the British during the <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_revolt_against_the_British" title="Iraqi revolt against the British">Iraqi revolt against the British</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Levies" class="mw-redirect" title="Assyrian Levies">Assyrian Levies</a> were founded by the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">British</a> in 1922, with ancient Assyrian military rankings, such as <a href="/wiki/Rab-shakeh" class="mw-redirect" title="Rab-shakeh">Rab-shakeh</a>, Rab-talia and Turtanu, being revived for the first time in millennia for this force. The Assyrians were prized by the British rulers for their fighting qualities, loyalty, bravery and discipline, and were used to help the British put down insurrections among the Arabs, Kurds and <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Turkman" class="mw-redirect" title="Iraqi Turkman">Turcoman</a>, guard the borders with Iran and Turkey, and protect British military installations. During the 1920s Assyrian levies saw action in effectively defeating Arab and Kurdish forces during anti-British rebellions in Iraq.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_136-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-136">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Simele_Massacre_and_World_War_II_.281930.E2.80.931950.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Simele_Massacre_and_World_War_II_(1930–1950)">Simele Massacre and World War II (1930–1950)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Simele Massacre and World War II (1930–1950)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Assyrian_populated_areas.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Map_of_Assyrian_populated_areas.svg/220px-Map_of_Assyrian_populated_areas.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Map_of_Assyrian_populated_areas.svg/330px-Map_of_Assyrian_populated_areas.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Map_of_Assyrian_populated_areas.svg/440px-Map_of_Assyrian_populated_areas.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="3435" data-file-height="2434" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Assyrian_populated_areas.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of Assyrian populated areas in Iraq, Syria, and Turkey</div></div></div>
<p>After Iraq was granted independence by the British in 1933, the Assyrians suffered the <a href="/wiki/Simele_Massacre" class="mw-redirect" title="Simele Massacre">Simele Massacre</a>, where thousands of unarmed villagers (men, women and children) were slaughtered by joint Arab-Kurdish forces of the Iraqi Army. The massacres of civilians followed a clash between armed Assyrian tribesmen and the Iraqi army, where the Iraqi forces suffered a defeat after trying to disarm the Assyrians, whom they feared would attempt to secede from Iraq. Armed <a href="/wiki/Iraq_Levies" title="Iraq Levies">Assyrian Levies</a> were prevented by the British from going to the aid of these civilians, and the British government then whitewashed the massacres at the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a>.
</p><p>Despite these betrayals, the Assyrians were allied with the British during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, with eleven Assyrian companies seeing action in <a href="/wiki/Mandatory_Palestine" title="Mandatory Palestine">Palestine</a>/<a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> and another four serving in Greece, Cyprus and Albania. Assyrians played a major role in the victory over Arab-Iraqi forces at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Habbaniya" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Habbaniya">Battle of Habbaniya</a> and elsewhere in 1941, when the Iraqi government decided to join World War II on the side of <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a>. The British presence in Iraq lasted until 1955, and Assyrian Levies remained attached to British forces until this time, after which they were disarmed and disbanded.
</p><p>A further persecution of Assyrians took place in the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> in the late 1940s and early 1950s when thousands of Assyrians settled in Georgia, Armenia and southern Russia were forcibly deported from their homes in the dead of night by <a href="/wiki/Stalin" class="mw-redirect" title="Stalin">Stalin</a> without warning or reason to <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>, with most being relocated to <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a>, where a small minority still remain.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Ba.27athism_.281966.E2.80.932003.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Ba'athism_(1966–2003)">Ba'athism (1966–2003)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Ba&#039;athism (1966–2003)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_Assyrians.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Flag_of_the_Assyrians.svg/220px-Flag_of_the_Assyrians.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Flag_of_the_Assyrians.svg/330px-Flag_of_the_Assyrians.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Flag_of_the_Assyrians.svg/440px-Flag_of_the_Assyrians.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_Assyrians.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_flag" title="Assyrian flag">Flag of the Assyrian Nation</a> (created and used since 1968)<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The period from the 1940s through to 1963 was a period of respite for the Assyrians in northern Iraq and north east Syria. The regime of Iraqi President Kassim in particular saw the Assyrians accepted into mainstream society. Many urban Assyrians became successful businessmen, a number of Assyrians moved south to cities such as Baghdad, <a href="/wiki/Basra" title="Basra">Basra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nasiriyah" title="Nasiriyah">Nasiriyah</a> to enhance their economic prospects, others were well represented in politics, the military, the arts and entertainment, Assyrian towns, villages, farmsteads and Assyrian quarters in major cities flourished undisturbed, and Assyrians came to excel and be over-represented in sports such as boxing, football, athletics, wrestling and swimming.
</p><p>However, in 1963, the <a href="/wiki/Ba%27ath_Party" title="Ba&#39;ath Party">Ba'ath Party</a> took power by force in Iraq, and came to power in Syria the same year. The Baathists, though secular, were <a href="/wiki/Arab_nationalism" title="Arab nationalism">Arab nationalists</a>, and set about attempting to <a href="/wiki/Arabization" title="Arabization">Arabize</a> the many non-Arab peoples of Iraq and Syria, including the Assyrians. This policy included refusing to acknowledge the Assyrians as an ethnic group, banning the publication of written material in Eastern Aramaic, and banning its teaching in schools, together with an attempt to <i>Arabize</i> the ancient pre-Arab heritage of Mesopotamian civilisation.
</p><p>The policies of the Baathists have also long been mirrored in Turkey, whose nationalist governments have refused to acknowledge the Assyrians as an ethnic group since the 1920s, and have attempted to <a href="/wiki/Turkification" title="Turkification">Turkify</a> the Assyrians by calling them "Semitic Turks" and forcing them to adopt Turkish names and language. In Iran, Assyrians continued to enjoy cultural, religious and ethnic rights, but due to the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Revolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic Revolution">Islamic Revolution</a> of 1979 their community has been diminished.
</p><p>In the aftermath of the <a href="/wiki/Iraq_War" title="Iraq War">Iraq War</a> of 2003, Assyrians became the targets of Islamist terrorist attacks and intimidation from both Sunni and Shia groups, as well as criminal kidnapping organisations; forcing many in southern and central Iraq to relocate to safer Assyrian regions in the north of the country or north east Syria.
</p>
<h4><span id="Kurdistan_Regional_Government_.282005.E2.80.93present.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Kurdistan_Regional_Government_(2005–present)">Kurdistan Regional Government (2005–present)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Kurdistan Regional Government (2005–present)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Kurdistan_Regional_Government" title="Kurdistan Regional Government">Kurdistan Regional Government</a> in <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Kurdistan" title="Iraqi Kurdistan">Iraqi Kurdistan</a> has been slowly trying to cause demographic changes in the <a href="/wiki/Nineveh_plains" class="mw-redirect" title="Nineveh plains">Nineveh plains</a>, where the vast majority of Assyrians in Iraq reside. The <a href="/wiki/Peshmerga" title="Peshmerga">Peshmerga</a> (KRG army), specifically <a href="/wiki/Kurdistan_Democratic_Party" title="Kurdistan Democratic Party">KDP</a>-affiliated Peshmerga, moved in and occupied the Nineveh plains in the absence of the <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Army" title="Iraqi Army">Iraqi Army</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Mosul" title="Fall of Mosul">Fall of Mosul</a>, the Peshmerga withdrew from the Nineveh plains while simultaneously disarming Assyrian residents, leaving local Assyrians vulnerable to ISIS attacks (which inevitably happened).<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> A couple of days later the Kurdish militia re-occupied the villages, an operation they portrayed as a liberation.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After the failed <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Kurdistan_independence_referendum,_2017" class="mw-redirect" title="Iraqi Kurdistan independence referendum, 2017">Iraqi Kurdistan independence referendum</a>, the KRG and the Iraqi federal government reached an agreement whereby the Peshmerga would withdraw from the Nineveh plains and the Iraqi army would take over. 
</p><p>In 2017, the KRG replaced the Alqosh mayor, Faiz Abed Jahwareh with a KDP member, Lara Yousif, and Assyrian protested in response.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>. The Iraqi Government ordered Lara Yousif to vacate her post, and return the title of Mayor to Jahwareh.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Syrian_Civil_War_.282012.E2.80.93present.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Syrian_Civil_War_(2012–present)">Syrian Civil War (2012–present)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Syrian Civil War (2012–present)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Genocide_of_Christians_by_ISIL" class="mw-redirect" title="Genocide of Christians by ISIL">Genocide of Christians by ISIL</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Assyrian_wedding,_Mechelen.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Assyrian_wedding%2C_Mechelen.jpg/220px-Assyrian_wedding%2C_Mechelen.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="211" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Assyrian_wedding%2C_Mechelen.jpg/330px-Assyrian_wedding%2C_Mechelen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Assyrian_wedding%2C_Mechelen.jpg/440px-Assyrian_wedding%2C_Mechelen.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2865" data-file-height="2751" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Assyrian_wedding,_Mechelen.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An Assyrian wedding in <a href="/wiki/Mechelen" title="Mechelen">Mechelen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In recent years, Assyrians in northern Iraq and north east Syria have become the target of attacks amounting to <a href="/wiki/Genocide" title="Genocide">genocide</a> by Islamist militants like <a href="/wiki/Islamic_State_of_Iraq_and_the_Levant" title="Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant">ISIL</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nusra_Front" class="mw-redirect" title="Nusra Front">Nusra Front</a>. In 2014, ISIL attacked Assyrian towns and villages in the <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_homeland" title="Assyrian homeland">Assyrian homelands</a> of northern Iraq and north east Syria, and Assyrians forced from their homes in cities such as Mosul had their houses and possessions stolen, both by ISIL and also by their own former Arab Muslim neighbours.<sup id="cite_ref-newyorknewsgrio.com_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newyorknewsgrio.com-149">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Assyrian <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> monuments and archaeological sites, as well as numerous Assyrian churches and monasteries,<sup id="cite_ref-newyorknewsgrio.com_149-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newyorknewsgrio.com-149">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> have been systematically vandalised and destroyed by ISIL. These include the ruins of <a href="/wiki/Nineveh" title="Nineveh">Nineveh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kalhu" class="mw-redirect" title="Kalhu">Kalhu</a> (<a href="/wiki/Nimrud" title="Nimrud">Nimrud</a>, <a href="/wiki/Assur" title="Assur">Assur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dur-Sharrukin" title="Dur-Sharrukin">Dur-Sharrukin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hatra" title="Hatra">Hatra</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> ISIL destroyed a 3,000 year-old <a href="/wiki/Ziggurat" title="Ziggurat">Ziggurat</a>. ISIL destroyed Virgin Mary Church, in 2015 St. Markourkas Church was destroyed and the cemetery was bulldozed.<sup id="cite_ref-Militia_in_Iraq_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Militia_in_Iraq-152">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Assyrians in both Iraq and Syria <sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> have responded by forming armed Assyrian militias to defend their territories,<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> and despite being heavily outnumbered and outgunned have had success in driving ISIL from Assyrian towns and villages, and defending others from attack.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> Armed Assyrian militias have also fought ISIL alongside armed groups of Kurds, Turcoman, Yezidis, Shabaks, Armenian Christians, Kawilya, Mandeans, Circassians and Shia Muslim Arabs and Iranians. <i>Dewkh Nawsha</i>, which translates to “those who sacrifice”, is a militia that was formed days after <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Mosul" title="Fall of Mosul">ISIL took over Mosul</a> in 2014. The military force is made up of volunteers, who come from all over the <a href="/wiki/Nineveh_Plains" title="Nineveh Plains">Nineveh Plains</a>. Dewkh Nawsha is supported by <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Patriotic_Party" title="Assyrian Patriotic Party">Assyrian Patriotic Party</a> and are led by Wilson Khammu.<sup id="cite_ref-Militia_in_Iraq_152-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Militia_in_Iraq-152">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It is estimated that nearly 60 percent of Iraqi Assyrians have fled. Assyrians who have fled have ended up all over the world. 2009 U.S Census Bureau survey, reported that roughly 100,000 have relocated to the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_culture" title="Assyrian culture">Assyrian culture</a></div>
<p>Assyria continued to exist as a geopolitical entity until the Arab-Islamic conquest in the mid-7th century. Assyrian identity; personal, family and tribal names; and both the spoken and written evolution of <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamian" class="mw-redirect" title="Mesopotamian">Mesopotamian</a> <a href="/wiki/Aramaic" title="Aramaic">Aramaic</a> (which still contains many <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">Akkadian</a> loan words and an Akkadian grammatical structure) have survived among the Assyrian people from ancient times to this day. An <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_calendar" title="Assyrian calendar">Assyrian calendar</a> has been revived.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Sumerian_language" title="Sumerian language">Sumerian language</a>, <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">Akkadian language</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aramaic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Aramaic language">Aramaic language</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Neo-Aramaic" title="Assyrian Neo-Aramaic">Assyrian Neo-Aramaic</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Frederick_Arthur_Bridgman,_The_Diversion_of_an_Assyrian_King._Oil_on_canvas._Sotheby%27s.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Frederick_Arthur_Bridgman%2C_The_Diversion_of_an_Assyrian_King._Oil_on_canvas._Sotheby%27s.jpg/220px-Frederick_Arthur_Bridgman%2C_The_Diversion_of_an_Assyrian_King._Oil_on_canvas._Sotheby%27s.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="109" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Frederick_Arthur_Bridgman%2C_The_Diversion_of_an_Assyrian_King._Oil_on_canvas._Sotheby%27s.jpg/330px-Frederick_Arthur_Bridgman%2C_The_Diversion_of_an_Assyrian_King._Oil_on_canvas._Sotheby%27s.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Frederick_Arthur_Bridgman%2C_The_Diversion_of_an_Assyrian_King._Oil_on_canvas._Sotheby%27s.jpg/440px-Frederick_Arthur_Bridgman%2C_The_Diversion_of_an_Assyrian_King._Oil_on_canvas._Sotheby%27s.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="987" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Frederick_Arthur_Bridgman,_The_Diversion_of_an_Assyrian_King._Oil_on_canvas._Sotheby%27s.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The pastime of an Assyrian King</i> by F.A. Bridgman</div></div></div>
<p>Emerging in <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumer</a> c. 3500 BC, <a href="/wiki/Cuneiform_writing" class="mw-redirect" title="Cuneiform writing">cuneiform writing</a> began as a system of <a href="/wiki/Pictograms" class="mw-redirect" title="Pictograms">pictograms</a>. Around 3000 BC, the pictorial representations became simplified and more abstract as the number of characters in use grew smaller. The original Sumerian script was adapted for the writing of the <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">Akkadian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">Assyrian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hittite_language" title="Hittite language">Hittite</a> languages.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/K%C3%BCltepe_texts" class="mw-redirect" title="Kültepe texts">Kültepe texts</a>, which were written in Old Assyrian, had <a href="/wiki/Hittite_language" title="Hittite language">Hittite</a> loanwords and names, which constitute the oldest record of any language of the <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_language_family" class="mw-redirect" title="Indo-European language family">Indo-European language family</a>. Most of the archaeological evidence is typical of <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a> rather than of Assyria, but the use of both <a href="/wiki/Cuneiform" title="Cuneiform">cuneiform</a> and the dialect is the best indication of Assyrian presence.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> From 1700 BC and onward, the <a href="/wiki/Sumerian_language" title="Sumerian language">Sumerian language</a> was preserved by the ancient <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonians</a> and Assyrians only as a <a href="/wiki/Liturgical_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Liturgical language">liturgical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Classical_language" title="Classical language">classical language</a> for religious, artistic and scholarly purposes.<sup id="cite_ref-woods_30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-woods-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Assyrian was a dialect of Akkadian, a member of the <a href="/wiki/East_Semitic_languages" title="East Semitic languages">eastern branch</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Semitic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Semitic language">Semitic</a> family and the oldest historically attested of the Semitic languages, which began to appear in written form in the 29th century BC. The first inscriptions in Assyria proper, called Old Assyrian (OA), were made in the Old Assyrian period.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> The ancient Assyrians also used Sumerian in their literature and liturgy,<sup id="cite_ref-Georges_Roux,_Ancient_Iraq_p382_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Georges_Roux,_Ancient_Iraq_p382-164">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> although to a more limited extent in the Middle- and Neo-Assyrian periods, when Akkadian became the main literary language.<sup id="cite_ref-Georges_Roux,_Ancient_Iraq_p382_164-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Georges_Roux,_Ancient_Iraq_p382-164">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the 3rd millennium BC, a very intimate cultural symbiosis developed between the Sumerians and Akkadian-speakers, which included widespread <a href="/wiki/Bilingualism" class="mw-redirect" title="Bilingualism">bilingualism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Deutscher_29-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Deutscher-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> The influence of Sumerian on Akkadian (and vice versa) is evident in all areas, from lexical borrowing on a massive scale, to syntactic, morphological, and phonological convergence.<sup id="cite_ref-Deutscher_29-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Deutscher-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> This has prompted scholars to refer to Sumerian and Akkadian in the 3rd millennium BC as a <i><a href="/wiki/Sprachbund" title="Sprachbund">Sprachbund</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Deutscher_29-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Deutscher-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Akkadian gradually replaced Sumerian as the spoken language of Mesopotamia somewhere around the turn of the 3rd and the 2nd millennium BC (the exact dating being a matter of debate),<sup id="cite_ref-woods_30-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-woods-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> but Sumerian continued to be used as a sacred, ceremonial, literary and scientific language in Mesopotamia until the 1st century AD.
</p><p>In the Neo-Assyrian period, the <a href="/wiki/Aramaic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Aramaic language">Aramaic language</a> became increasingly common,<sup id="cite_ref-Roux_1964_308_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roux_1964_308-165">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> more so than Akkadian—this was thought to be largely due to the mass deportations undertaken by Assyrian kings,<sup id="cite_ref-Georges_Roux,_Ancient_Iraq_p382_164-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Georges_Roux,_Ancient_Iraq_p382-164">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> in which large Aramaic-speaking populations, conquered by the Assyrians, were relocated to Assyria and interbred with the Assyrians, and due to the fact that <a href="/wiki/Tiglath-pileser_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Tiglath-pileser II">Tiglath-pileser II</a> made it the <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a> of Assyria and its empire in the 8th century BC. The destruction of the Assyrian capitals of Nineveh and Assur by the Babylonians, Medes and their allies, ensured that much of the bilingual elite (but not all) were wiped out. By the 7th century BC, much of the Assyrian population used distinct Akkadian-influenced <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Aramaic_languages" title="Eastern Aramaic languages">Eastern Aramaic varieties</a> and not Akkadian itself. The last Akkadian inscriptions in Mesopotamia date from the 1st century AD. The <a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">Syriac language</a> also emerged in Assyria during the 5th century BC, and during the Christian era, <a href="/wiki/Syriac_literature" title="Syriac literature">Syriac literature</a> and <a href="/wiki/Syriac_script" class="mw-redirect" title="Syriac script">Syriac script</a> were to become hugely influential.
</p><p>However, the descendant Akkadian-influenced Eastern Aramaic dialects from the Neo-Assyrian Empire, as well as Akkadian and Mesopotamian Aramaic personal, tribal, family and place names, still survive to this day among <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">Assyrian people</a> and are spoken fluently by up to 1,000,000 Assyrians, with a further number having lesser and varying degrees of fluency.<sup id="cite_ref-Georges_Roux,_Ancient_Iraq_p382_164-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Georges_Roux,_Ancient_Iraq_p382-164">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> These dialects which contain many Akkadian loan words and grammatical features are very different from the now almost extinct <a href="/wiki/Western_Aramaic" class="mw-redirect" title="Western Aramaic">Western Aramaic</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Arameans" title="Arameans">Arameans</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transjordan_(region)" title="Transjordan (region)">Trans-Jordan</a>, which does not have any Akkadian grammatical structure or loan words.
</p><p>After 90 years of effort, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Chicago" title="University of Chicago">University of Chicago</a> in 2011 completed an Assyrian dictionary, the style of which is more like an encyclopedia than a dictionary.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_Assyrian_religion">Ancient Assyrian religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Ancient Assyrian religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Mesopotamian_religion" title="Ancient Mesopotamian religion">Ancient Mesopotamian religion</a></div>
<p>The Assyrians, like the rest of the Mesopotamian peoples, followed <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Mesopotamian_religion" title="Ancient Mesopotamian religion">ancient Mesopotamian religion</a>, with their national god <a href="/wiki/Ashur_(god)" title="Ashur (god)">Ashur</a> having the most importance to them during the Assyrian Empire. This religion gradually declined with the advent of <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Christianity" title="Syriac Christianity">Syriac Christianity</a> between the first and tenth centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-nineveh.com_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nineveh.com-100">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The major deities worshipped in Assyria include;
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Adad" class="mw-redirect" title="Adad">Adad</a> (<a href="/wiki/Hadad" title="Hadad">Hadad</a>) – storm and rain god</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Anu" title="Anu">Anu</a> or An – god of heaven and the sky, lord of constellations, and father of the gods. The name is derived from Sumero-Akkadian/ana/, which means heaven; He is considered the father of great gods. In stories, he is mentioned as a father, creator, and god; and is believed to be the supreme being.<sup id="cite_ref-Pinches_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pinches-167">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dagan_(god)" class="mw-redirect" title="Dagan (god)">Dagan</a> or <a href="/wiki/Dagon" title="Dagon">Dagon</a> – god of fertility</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Enki" title="Enki">Enki</a> or Ea – god of the <a href="/wiki/Abzu" title="Abzu">Abzu</a>, crafts, water, intelligence, mischief and creation and divine ruler of the Earth and its humans</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ereshkigal" title="Ereshkigal">Ereshkigal</a> – goddess of <a href="/wiki/Irkalla" class="mw-redirect" title="Irkalla">Irkalla</a>, the Underworld</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ishtar" class="mw-redirect" title="Ishtar">Ishtar</a> or <a href="/wiki/Inanna" title="Inanna">Inanna</a>/<a href="/wiki/Astarte" title="Astarte">Astarte</a> – goddess of fertility, love, and war</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Marduk" title="Marduk">Marduk</a> – patron deity of <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a> who eventually became regarded as the head of the Babylonian pantheon</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nabu" title="Nabu">Nabu</a> – god of wisdom and writing</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nanshe" title="Nanshe">Nanshe</a> – goddess of prophecy, fertility and fishing</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nergal" title="Nergal">Nergal</a> – god of plague, war, and the sun in its destructive capacity; later husband of Ereshkigal</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ninhursag" title="Ninhursag">Ninhursag</a> or <a href="/wiki/Mami_(goddess)" title="Mami (goddess)">Mami</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belet-Ili" class="mw-redirect" title="Belet-Ili">Belet-Ili</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ki_(goddess)" title="Ki (goddess)">Ki</a>, Ninmah, Nintu, or Aruru – earth and <a href="/wiki/Mother_goddess" title="Mother goddess">mother goddess</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ninlil" title="Ninlil">Ninlil</a> – goddess of the air; consort of Enlil</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ninurta" title="Ninurta">Ninurta</a> – champion of the gods, the epitome of youthful vigour, and god of agriculture</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nisroch" title="Nisroch">Nisroch</a> – god of agriculture. Some other religions also consider him the fallen angel or demon.<sup id="cite_ref-Pinches_167-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pinches-167">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nusku" class="mw-redirect" title="Nusku">Nusku</a> – The messenger for the Gods. “"the offspring of the abyss, the creation of Êa," and "the likeness of his father, the first-born of Bel." Nusku was also considered a great commander, counselor of the gods, and protector of gods in heaven. Assyrian kings mention Nusku many times, especially before wars; Nusku was fearless in battle.<sup id="cite_ref-Pinches_167-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pinches-167">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Shamash" class="mw-redirect" title="Shamash">Shamash</a> or <a href="/wiki/Utu" title="Utu">Utu</a> – god of the sun, arbiter of justice and patron of travellers</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sin_(mythology)" title="Sin (mythology)">Sin</a> or Nanna – god of the moon. Considered to be the prince of the gods. Described as having a perfect body: everything from beard to horns is perfect. The name is believed to come from "Zu-ena" but was changed at some point. Zu-ena means “knowledge-lord”. Sin is also mentioned in other religions in Babylonia<sup id="cite_ref-Pinches_167-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pinches-167">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tammuz_(deity)" class="mw-redirect" title="Tammuz (deity)">Tammuz</a> or Dumuzi – god of food and vegetation</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tiamat" title="Tiamat">Tiamat</a></li></ul>
<p>The original, polytheistic religion of the Assyrians was widely adhered to until around the 4th century, and survived in pockets until at least the 10th century.<sup id="cite_ref-nineveh.com_100-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nineveh.com-100">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> However, Assyrians today are exclusively Christian, with most following the <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Church_of_the_East" title="Assyrian Church of the East">Assyrian Church of the East</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chaldean_Catholic_Church" title="Chaldean Catholic Church">Chaldean Catholic Church</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Church_of_the_East" title="Ancient Church of the East">Ancient Church of the East</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Orthodox_Church" title="Syriac Orthodox Church">Syriac Orthodox Church</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Catholic_Church" title="Syriac Catholic Church">Syriac Catholic Church</a>, <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Pentecostal_Church" title="Assyrian Pentecostal Church">Assyrian Pentecostal Church</a> and <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Evangelical_Church" title="Assyrian Evangelical Church">Assyrian Evangelical Church</a>. Assyrians had begun to adopt Christianity (as well as for a time <a href="/wiki/Manicheanism" class="mw-redirect" title="Manicheanism">Manicheanism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gnosticism" title="Gnosticism">gnosticism</a>) between the 1st and 3rd centuries AD.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Christianity">Christianity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Christianity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Asia_Minor_in_the_early_1st_century_AD_-_general_map_-_provinces,_client_states_and_main_settlements_-_bleached_-_English_legend.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Asia_Minor_in_the_early_1st_century_AD_-_general_map_-_provinces%2C_client_states_and_main_settlements_-_bleached_-_English_legend.jpg/220px-Asia_Minor_in_the_early_1st_century_AD_-_general_map_-_provinces%2C_client_states_and_main_settlements_-_bleached_-_English_legend.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Asia_Minor_in_the_early_1st_century_AD_-_general_map_-_provinces%2C_client_states_and_main_settlements_-_bleached_-_English_legend.jpg/330px-Asia_Minor_in_the_early_1st_century_AD_-_general_map_-_provinces%2C_client_states_and_main_settlements_-_bleached_-_English_legend.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Asia_Minor_in_the_early_1st_century_AD_-_general_map_-_provinces%2C_client_states_and_main_settlements_-_bleached_-_English_legend.jpg/440px-Asia_Minor_in_the_early_1st_century_AD_-_general_map_-_provinces%2C_client_states_and_main_settlements_-_bleached_-_English_legend.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6167" data-file-height="4027" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Asia_Minor_in_the_early_1st_century_AD_-_general_map_-_provinces,_client_states_and_main_settlements_-_bleached_-_English_legend.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Osroene (Mesopotamia) in the first century</div></div></div>
<p>The tradition of the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_East" title="Church of the East">Church of the East</a> is that <a href="/wiki/Thomas_the_Apostle" title="Thomas the Apostle">Thomas the Apostle</a> and his disciples Addai (<a href="/wiki/Thaddeus_of_Edessa" title="Thaddeus of Edessa">Thaddeus of Edessa</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Saint_Mari" title="Saint Mari">Mari</a> brought Christianity to <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, thus attributing to the first century the founding of the <a href="/wiki/Episcopal_see" title="Episcopal see">episcopal see</a> of <a href="/wiki/Seleucia-Ctesiphon" class="mw-redirect" title="Seleucia-Ctesiphon">Seleucia-Ctesiphon</a>, which became that Church's primatial see in 410. There is clear evidence of the presence of Christianity in <a href="/wiki/Osroene" title="Osroene">Osroene</a> in the second century. At that time, Christians were persecuted in the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>, but were at peace under the expanding <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Persian Empire</a>. <a href="/wiki/Shapur_I" title="Shapur I">Shapur I</a> (241–272), the second <i>shahinshah</i> (king of kings) of the Sasanian dynasty, occupied Roman territory, advancing as far as <a href="/wiki/Antioch" title="Antioch">Antioch</a> in 260, and deported eastward much of the population to strengthen the economy of his own empire. One of those deported in 253 was Bishop <a href="/wiki/Demetrius_of_Antioch" title="Demetrius of Antioch">Demetrius of Antioch</a>, who then became the first bishop of <a href="/wiki/Gundeshapur" title="Gundeshapur">Beth Lapat</a>. After 312, when <a href="/wiki/Constantine_the_Great" title="Constantine the Great">Constantine the Great</a> legalized Christianity in the Roman Empire, Christians in Persia came under suspicion of pro-Roman sympathies and were persecuted, especially under <a href="/wiki/Shapur_II" title="Shapur II">Shapur II</a> (309–379).<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Under <a href="/wiki/Yazdegerd_I" title="Yazdegerd I">Yazdegerd I</a> (399–421) the situation of the Christian minority improved considerably. In 410, on the recommendation of several Western bishops (the signatories included the bishops of <a href="/wiki/Antioch" title="Antioch">Antioch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aleppo" title="Aleppo">Aleppo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edessa" title="Edessa">Edessa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amid" class="mw-redirect" title="Amid">Amid</a>) Yazdegerd called the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Seleucia-Ctesiphon" title="Council of Seleucia-Ctesiphon">Council of Seleucia-Ctesiphon</a>, which organized the Persian Church after the model approved by the <a href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Nicea" class="mw-redirect" title="First Council of Nicea">First Council of Nicea</a> for the Church in the Roman Empire. The <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_East" title="Church of the East">Church of the East</a> was arranged as six <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastical_province" title="Ecclesiastical province">ecclesiastical provinces</a>, with the bishops in each grouped around a <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_bishop" title="Metropolitan bishop">metropolitan</a>, while the bishop of Seleucia-Ctesiphon, the <a href="/wiki/Capital_city" title="Capital city">capital city</a>, referred to in the acts of the council as the Grand Metropolitan, held authority throughout the Church and for that reason was called (probably only from a later date) the <a href="/wiki/Catholicos" title="Catholicos">Catholicos</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Papa_bar_Aggai" class="mw-redirect" title="Papa bar Aggai">Papa bar Aggai</a>, who in about 315, almost 100 years before this council, suffered a sudden stroke during a synod held to depose him, is looked on as the first Catholicos of the Church of the East, although this may only mean that he was the first bishop of Seleucia-Ctesiphon.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In a synod held in Markabta in 424, the participating bishops recalled the circumstances concerning Papa, blaming the opposition to him on the influence of unnamed Western bishops, and declared or reaffirmed that the Catholicos of Seleucia-Ctesiphon was totally independent. They excluded any right of appeal against him to any patriarch in the West.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> They "defined, by the word of God, that Easterners cannot appeal to Western patriarchs against their patriarch. Any case that cannot be resolved in his presence shall be reserved to the tribunal of Christ [...] There can be no reason for thinking or saying that the Catholicos of the East can be judged by superiors or by another patriarch. He himself is to be the judge of all his subjects, and judgment on himself is reserved to Christ, who has chosen him, raised him up and placed him at the head of his Church."<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>This was six years before the 431 <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Ephesus" title="Council of Ephesus">Council of Ephesus</a>, the enforcement within the Byzantine Empire of whose condemnation of <a href="/wiki/Nestorianism" title="Nestorianism">Nestorianism</a> is sometimes given as what led to the break between the Church of the East and the Western Churches.
</p><p>In 484, Catholicos <a href="/wiki/Babowai" title="Babowai">Babowai</a> wrote to some Western bishops asking them to get the Byzantine emperor to intercede with the Persian king <a href="/wiki/Peroz_I" title="Peroz I">Peroz I</a> on behalf of persecuted Christians.
