His letter was intercepted, reportedly by <a href="/wiki/Barsauma" title="Barsauma">Barsauma</a>, metropolitan of <a href="/wiki/Nisibis" class="mw-redirect" title="Nisibis">Nisibis</a>, between whom and Babowai there was a heated dispute. It was shown to the king, who then had Babowai executed. Barsauma called the <a href="/wiki/Synod_of_Beth_Lapat" title="Synod of Beth Lapat">Synod of Beth Lapat</a>, which, as well as condemning some of Babowai's policies, permitted marriage of clergy and of vowed monks and reputedly adopted Nestorian teaching. Under Babowai's successor, <a href="/wiki/Acacius_of_Seleucia-Ctesiphon" title="Acacius of Seleucia-Ctesiphon">Acacius of Seleucia-Ctesiphon</a>, a synod held in the capital in 486 revoked the decrees of the Synod of <a href="/wiki/Gundeshapur" title="Gundeshapur">Beth Lapat</a>, whose acts have consequently not been preserved, and in its own name affirmed the teaching of <a href="/wiki/Theodore_of_Mopsuestia" title="Theodore of Mopsuestia">Theodore of Mopsuestia</a> against <a href="/wiki/Monophysitism" title="Monophysitism">Monophysitism</a>, forbade wandering monks or clergy, and allowed marriage of clergy and monks.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Roman-Persian_Frontier_in_Late_Antiquity.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Roman-Persian_Frontier_in_Late_Antiquity.svg/220px-Roman-Persian_Frontier_in_Late_Antiquity.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Roman-Persian_Frontier_in_Late_Antiquity.svg/330px-Roman-Persian_Frontier_in_Late_Antiquity.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Roman-Persian_Frontier_in_Late_Antiquity.svg/440px-Roman-Persian_Frontier_in_Late_Antiquity.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="2710" data-file-height="1740" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Roman-Persian_Frontier_in_Late_Antiquity.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Roman-Persian frontier in the late fourth and late sixth century</div></div></div>
<p>In 489, the Eastern Roman Emperor <a href="/wiki/Zeno_(emperor)" title="Zeno (emperor)">Zeno</a> closed the theological <a href="/wiki/School_of_Edessa" title="School of Edessa">school of Edessa</a> because of its promotion of the teaching of Theodore of Mopsuestia. Barsauma welcomed its teachers and revived the <a href="/wiki/School_of_Nisibis" title="School of Nisibis">school of Nisibis</a>. A century later, an attempt by the school's director to include influences other than that of Theodore alone<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> His initiative was opposed by <a href="/wiki/Babai_the_Great" title="Babai the Great">Babai the Great</a> (551–628), whose exposition of the theology of Theodore of Mopsuestia became the official teaching of the Church of the East.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At this time <a href="/wiki/Miaphysitism" title="Miaphysitism">miaphysitism</a> was advancing in the Persian Empire. Its followers were mainly from the "hundreds of thousands" of Western Syriac Christians whom <a href="/wiki/Khosrow_I" title="Khosrow I">Khosrow I</a> (531–579) and <a href="/wiki/Khosrow_II" title="Khosrow II">Khosrow II</a> (590 and 591–628) deported to their own territory, as well as descendants of those previously deported, but there were also some defectors from the local Church of the East.<sup id="cite_ref-Varghese_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Varghese-187">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EncRel_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EncRel-188">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> In addition, West Syrian opponents of the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Chalcedon" title="Council of Chalcedon">Council of Chalcedon</a> sought refuge in Persia from the pro-Chalcedonian policy of Emperors <a href="/wiki/Justin_I" title="Justin I">Justin I</a> and <a href="/wiki/Justinian_I" title="Justinian I">Justinian I</a> and actively propagated their own theology.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Baradaeus" title="Jacob Baradaeus">Jacob Baradaeus</a>, who was ordained as Bishop of <a href="/wiki/Edessa" title="Edessa">Edessa</a> in about 543, set about ordaining bishops and priests throughout the Syriac-speaking areas of West Asia to such an extent that he was even claimed to have ordained over 100,000 clergy and nearly 30 bishops. Whatever the number, he set up a church structure parallel to and independent of that approved by the Byzantine emperors, so that the <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Orthodox_Church" title="Syriac Orthodox Church">Syriac Orthodox Church</a> has been called Jacobite in reference to him.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> For Miaphysites in Persia, particularly strong in <a href="/wiki/Tikrit" title="Tikrit">Tagrit</a>, he in 559 appointed as "metropolitan of the East" <a href="/wiki/Ahudemmeh" title="Ahudemmeh">Ahudemmeh</a>, a convert from the Church of the East, who won from Khosrow I freedom of worship for the Miaphysites (unlike the Chalcedonian Christians).<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> Ahudemmeh made many converts among the Arabs.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> The Miaphysites of Persia united with the Syriac Orthodox Church, and in 629 Patriarch <a href="/wiki/Athanasius_I_Gammolo" title="Athanasius I Gammolo">Athanasius I Gammolo</a> placed at their head <a href="/wiki/Marutha_of_Tikrit" title="Marutha of Tikrit">Marutha of Tagrit</a> with the title of <a href="/wiki/Maphrian" title="Maphrian">Maphrian</a> and a wide-ranging autonomy that would allay Persian suspicion that, as spiritual subjects of a patriarch who lived under Byzantine rule, the Miaphysites would tend to be disloyal.<sup id="cite_ref-Varghese_187-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Varghese-187">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dioceses_of_the_Church_of_the_East.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Dioceses_of_the_Church_of_the_East.svg/220px-Dioceses_of_the_Church_of_the_East.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="183" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Dioceses_of_the_Church_of_the_East.svg/330px-Dioceses_of_the_Church_of_the_East.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Dioceses_of_the_Church_of_the_East.svg/440px-Dioceses_of_the_Church_of_the_East.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="875" data-file-height="727" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dioceses_of_the_Church_of_the_East.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Metropolitan sees and missionary activity of the Church of the East in the Middle Ages</div></div></div>
<p>Weakened by their long <a href="/wiki/Byzantine%E2%80%93Sasanian_War_of_602%E2%80%93628" title="Byzantine–Sasanian War of 602–628">struggle against the Byzantines</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> the Persians were unable to withstand the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Persia" title="Muslim conquest of Persia">Arab conquest</a>. Seleucia-Ctesiphon fell in 637. The last Persian king <a href="/wiki/Yazdegerd_III" title="Yazdegerd III">Yazdegerd III</a> became a fugitive and was murdered for his money in 651/2.
</p><p>For Christians in Persia, the change from <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrian</a> to <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islamic</a> rulers did not worsen their situation, but rather bettered it, especially for the "Nestorians" (East Syrians).<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> This was a time of increased missionary activity by the Church of the East, whose success in China with the missionary <a href="/wiki/Alopen" title="Alopen">Alopen</a> is attested by the <a href="/wiki/Nestorian_Stele" class="mw-redirect" title="Nestorian Stele">Nestorian Stele</a> and in India by the continued maintenance of its liturgy by the <a href="/wiki/Syro-Malabar_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Syro-Malabar Church">Syro-Malabar Church</a>. The patriarchate of <a href="/wiki/Timothy_I_(Nestorian_patriarch)" title="Timothy I (Nestorian patriarch)">Timothy I</a> (780–823) was a high point of the Church's expansion.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After the general destruction wrought by <a href="/wiki/Genghis_Khan" title="Genghis Khan">Genghis Khan</a>, the Church of the East fared no worse under the Mongols of the <a href="/wiki/Ilkhanate" title="Ilkhanate">Ilkhanate</a> than under the Arabs, but at the end of the 14th century <a href="/wiki/Timur" title="Timur">Timur</a> brought disaster on it,<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> exterminating it in many regions,<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> so that it survived only in the Kurdistan mountains and in India.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>An account of the divisions within the Church of the East from the mid-16th to the early 19th century is given <a href="#Breakup_of_the_Church_of_the_East_(1552–1830)">above</a>. The separate patriarchates at one stage grew to four, but were reduced in 1830 to two: the now more numerous <a href="/wiki/Chaldean_Catholic_Church" title="Chaldean Catholic Church">Chaldean Catholic Church</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Church_of_the_East" title="Assyrian Church of the East">Assyrian Church of the East</a>. The latter was further divided in the 20th century, with a split between the Assyrian Church of the East and the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Church_of_the_East" title="Ancient Church of the East">Ancient Church of the East</a> over reforms by <a href="/wiki/Shimun_XXI_Eshai" title="Shimun XXI Eshai">Shimun XXI Eshai</a> in the 1960s.
</p><p>After the Arab conquest had removed the previously existing frontier between the Byzantine and Persian Empires, the Syriac Orthodox Church no longer needed to maintain a clear distinction between the part under the direct rule of the Patriarch and the part in the care of the Maphrian. From 793 the Maphrian was no longer elected by the Eastern bishops but simply appointed by the Patriarch. The Maphrianate thus became, until abolished in 1860, a mere title for the second in dignity within the Church. The Church itself, like that of the East, underwent divisions. William Taylor states that for 475 years, from 1364 to 1839, there were two rival series of Patriarchs, one in <a href="/wiki/Mardin" title="Mardin">Mardin</a>, the other in <a href="/wiki/Tur_Abdin" title="Tur Abdin">Tur Abdin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1665 the Syrian Orthodox Church won the allegiance of about a third of the <a href="/wiki/Saint_Thomas_Christians" title="Saint Thomas Christians">Saint Thomas Christians</a> in southwestern India, whose traditional liturgy had been that of the Church of the East.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> However, due to Anglican influence, they lost many of these in the 19th and 20th centuries through the setting up of the more <a href="/wiki/Evangelicalism" title="Evangelicalism">Evangelical</a> <a href="/wiki/Mar_Thoma_Syrian_Church" title="Mar Thoma Syrian Church">Mar Thoma Syrian Church</a> and <a href="/wiki/St._Thomas_Evangelical_Church" title="St. Thomas Evangelical Church">St. Thomas Evangelical Church</a> and about half of those remaining in the 20th century declared their Church (the <a href="/wiki/Malankara_Orthodox_Syrian_Church" title="Malankara Orthodox Syrian Church">Malankara Orthodox Syrian Church</a>) <a href="/wiki/Autocephaly" title="Autocephaly">autocephalous</a>, while those remaining in obedience to the Patriarch (the <a href="/wiki/Jacobite_Syrian_Christian_Church" title="Jacobite Syrian Christian Church">Jacobite Syrian Christian Church</a>) have been granted autonomy within the Syrian Orthodox Church such as was once granted to the Maphran-headed part of the Church in Persia.
</p><p>At about the same time as the Syriac Orthodox Church was expanding into India, where now three quarters of its membership live,<sup id="cite_ref-pro-oriente.at_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pro-oriente.at-204">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Order_of_Friars_Minor_Capuchin" title="Order of Friars Minor Capuchin">Capuchin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jesuit" class="mw-redirect" title="Jesuit">Jesuit</a> missionaries won to union with Rome the majority of the Syriac Orthodox in <a href="/wiki/Aleppo" title="Aleppo">Aleppo</a>, including, in 1656, their bishop, <a href="/wiki/Ignatius_Andrew_Akijan" title="Ignatius Andrew Akijan">Andrew Akijan</a>, who in 1662 was elected Patriarch of the Syriac Orthodox Church. On his death in 1677, two strong factions emerged, each of which elected a Patriarch, one pro-, the other anti-Rome. The Ottoman civil authorities recognized the non-Catholic Patriarch and suppressed the Catholic faction, eventually forcing it underground. In 1782 the newly elected Syriac Orthodox Patriarch declared himself Catholic and moved to Lebanon. He was replaced as Patriarch of the Syriac Orthodox Church, but initiated a series of Catholic patriarchs that in 1828 was recognized by the Ottoman authorities as heading a distinct Catholic Syriac Church. In 1850, the Catholic patriarchal seat was moved to <a href="/wiki/Mardin" title="Mardin">Mardin</a>. Many of its faithful were massacred during the <a href="/wiki/First_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="First World War">First World War</a>. The patriarchal seat is now Beirut, where it was moved in the 1920.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Patriarch <a href="/wiki/Ignatius_Peter_IV" title="Ignatius Peter IV">Ignatius Peter IV</a> (1872–1894) made an attempt in 1889 to set up a Latin-rite branch of his Syriac Orthodox Church by having the <a href="/wiki/Goa" title="Goa">Goan</a> <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Francisco_Xavier_Alvares" title="Antonio Francisco Xavier Alvares">Antonio Francisco Xavier Alvares</a> ordained, with the religious name of Mar Julius I, as Archbishop of Ceylon, Goa and India. In May 1892, Alvares, with the consent of the Patriarch, ordained <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Vilatte" title="René Vilatte">René Vilatte</a> as Archbishop of America. In later years Vilatte consecrated "a number of men who are the episcopal ancestors of an enormous variety of descendants" in what is called the <a href="/wiki/Independent_sacramental_movement" title="Independent sacramental movement">independent sacramental movement</a> or <a href="/wiki/Independent_Catholicism" title="Independent Catholicism">independent Catholicism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1933, the seat of the patriarchate of the Syriac Orthodox Church was moved from the "Saffron Monastery" (<a href="/wiki/Mor_Hananyo_Monastery" title="Mor Hananyo Monastery">Mor Hananyo Monastery</a>) of <a href="/wiki/Tur_Abdin" title="Tur Abdin">Tur Abdin</a>, 4 kilometres north of <a href="/wiki/Mardin" title="Mardin">Mardin</a>, Turkey to <a href="/wiki/Homs" title="Homs">Homs</a>, Syria and in 1959 to <a href="/wiki/Bab_Tuma" title="Bab Tuma">Bab Tuma</a> (literally meaning "Thomas Gate"), <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a>, capital of Syria; but the Patriarch actually resides at the Mar Aphrem Monastery in <a href="/wiki/Maarat_Saidnaya" title="Maarat Saidnaya">Maarat Saidnaya</a>, about 25 kilometres north of Damascus.<sup id="cite_ref-EncRel_188-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EncRel-188">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Syriac Orthodox Church has today about 2 million followers, three quarters of whom belong to the autonomous Jacobite Syrian Christian Church in India.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> The Syriac Catholic Church has about 160,000 faithful, some 65,000 of them in Syria, 55,000 in Iraq, as well as about 15,000 in Lebanon and the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-pro-oriente.at_204-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pro-oriente.at-204">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2009 study by Sargon Donabed and Shamiran Mako cites the remark made by <a href="/wiki/Horatio_Southgate" title="Horatio Southgate">Horatio Southgate</a>, on learning that the Armenians called the <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Orthodox_Church" title="Syriac Orthodox Church">Syrians</a> <i>Assouri</i> (not <i>Asorestants’i</i>, the Armenian word for Assyrian), that the Syrians call themselves sons of Asshur.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> They also mention a dispute in 1939 between a Syrian Orthodox writer from Mosul who protested against application to his co-religionists of the name "Assyrians" and the editor of a publication that supported it.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> They say that the rejection of the "Assyrian" label in favour of "Syrian" or "Aramean" was promoted by the church and later became prevalent in modern scholarship.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup> Thus J.F. Coakley described as "bogus ethnology" the "Assyrians" description.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> Donabeg and Mako deplore and argue against this judgment and that of other academics and attribute its prevalence in part to political considerations.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The continuing trend towards identification as Arameans is evidenced also in the government of Israel's recognition in September 2014 of the <a href="/wiki/Arameans_in_Israel" title="Arameans in Israel">Arameans in Israel</a> as a distinct nationality.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_Mesopotamia" title="Architecture of Mesopotamia">Architecture of Mesopotamia</a></div>
<p>Assyrian architecture, like that of Babylonia, was influenced by Sumero-Akkadian styles (and to some degree Mitanni), but early on developed its own distinctive style. Palaces sported colourful wall decorations, and seal-cutting (an art learned from <a href="/wiki/Mittani" class="mw-redirect" title="Mittani">Mittani</a>) developed apace. Schools for scribes taught both the Babylonian and Assyrian dialects of Akkadian, and Sumerian and Akkadian literary works were often copied with an Assyrian flavour.
</p><p>The Assyrian dialect of Akkadian was used in legal, official, religious, and practical texts such as medicine or instructions on manufacturing items. During the 13th to 10th centuries, picture tales appeared as a new art form: a continuous series of images carved on square stone steles. Somewhat reminiscent of a comic book, these show events such as warfare or hunting, placed in order from the upper left to the lower right corner of the stele with captions written underneath them. These and the excellent cut seals show that Assyrian art was beginning to surpass that of Babylon. Architecture saw the introduction of a new style of <a href="/wiki/Ziggurat" title="Ziggurat">ziggurat</a>, with two towers and colorful enameled tiles.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arts_and_Sciences">Arts and Sciences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Arts and Sciences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Art_of_Mesopotamia" title="Art of Mesopotamia">Art of Mesopotamia</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Assyrian_Winged_Bull.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Assyrian_Winged_Bull.jpg/275px-Assyrian_Winged_Bull.jpg" decoding="async" width="275" height="367" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Assyrian_Winged_Bull.jpg/413px-Assyrian_Winged_Bull.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Assyrian_Winged_Bull.jpg/550px-Assyrian_Winged_Bull.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3024" data-file-height="4032" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Assyrian_Winged_Bull.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <i><a href="/wiki/Lamassu" title="Lamassu">Lamassu</a></i>, from the entrance into the kings private apartments; 865–860 BC; <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a> (London)</div></div></div>
<p>Assyrian art preserved to the present day predominantly dates to the Neo-Assyrian period. Art depicting battle scenes, and occasionally the impaling of whole villages in gory detail, was intended to show the power of the emperor, and was generally made for propaganda purposes. These stone reliefs lined the walls in the royal palaces where foreigners were received by the king. Other stone reliefs depict the king with different deities and conducting religious ceremonies. Many stone reliefs were discovered in the royal palaces at <a href="/wiki/Nimrud" title="Nimrud">Nimrud</a> (Kalhu) and <a href="/wiki/Khorsabad" class="mw-redirect" title="Khorsabad">Khorsabad</a> (Dur-Sharrukin). A rare discovery of metal plates belonging to wooden doors was made at <a href="/wiki/Balawat" title="Balawat">Balawat</a> (<a href="/wiki/Imgur-Enlil" class="mw-redirect" title="Imgur-Enlil">Imgur-Enlil</a>).
</p><p>Assyrian sculpture reached a high level of refinement in the Neo-Assyrian period. One prominent example is the winged bull <a href="/wiki/Lamassu" title="Lamassu">lamassu</a> or <a href="/wiki/Shedu" class="mw-redirect" title="Shedu">shedu</a> that guard the entrances to the king's court. These were apotropaic meaning they were intended to ward off evil. C.W. Ceram states in <i>The March of Archaeology</i> that lamassi were typically sculpted with five legs so that four legs were always visible, whether the image were viewed frontally or in profile.
</p><p>Although works of precious gems and metals usually do not survive the ravages of time, some fine pieces of Assyrian jewelry were found in royal tombs at Nimrud.
</p><p>There is ongoing discussion among academics over the nature of the <a href="/wiki/Nimrud_lens" title="Nimrud lens">Nimrud lens</a>, a piece of <a href="/wiki/Quartz" title="Quartz">quartz</a> unearthed by <a href="/wiki/Austen_Henry_Layard" title="Austen Henry Layard">Austen Henry Layard</a> in 1850, in the Nimrud palace complex in northern Iraq. A small minority believe that it is evidence for the existence of ancient Assyrian telescopes, which could explain the great accuracy of <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_astronomy" class="mw-redirect" title="Assyrian astronomy">Assyrian astronomy</a>. Other suggestions include its use as a magnifying glass for jewellers, or as a decorative furniture inlay. The Nimrud Lens is held in the British Museum.<sup id="cite_ref-Muſeum_216-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Muſeum-216">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Assyrians were also innovative in military technology, with the use of heavy cavalry, sappers and siege engines.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Assyria" title="Achaemenid Assyria">Achaemenid Assyria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Assyriology" title="Assyriology">Assyriology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_nationalism" title="Assyrian nationalism">Assyrian nationalism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Layard_Nineveh.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Layard_Nineveh.jpg/220px-Layard_Nineveh.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="132" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Layard_Nineveh.jpg/330px-Layard_Nineveh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Layard_Nineveh.jpg/440px-Layard_Nineveh.jpg 2x" data-file-width="598" data-file-height="360" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Layard_Nineveh.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Austen_Henry_Layard" title="Austen Henry Layard">Austen Henry Layard</a> in <a href="/wiki/Nineveh" title="Nineveh">Nineveh</a>, 1852</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Assyria" title="Achaemenid Assyria">Achaemenid Assyria</a> (539–330 BC) retained a separate identity, official correspondence being in <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Aramaic" class="mw-redirect" title="Imperial Aramaic">Imperial Aramaic</a>, and there was even a determined revolt of the two Assyrian provinces of Mada and Athura in 520 BC. Under Seleucid rule, however, Aramaic gave way to <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> as the official administrative language. Aramaic was marginalised as an official language, but remained spoken in both Assyria and Babylonia by the general populace. It also remained the spoken tongue of the indigenous Assyrian/Babylonian citizens of all Mesopotamia under Persian, Greek and Roman rule, and indeed well into the Arab period it was still the language of the majority, particularly in the north of Mesopotamia, surviving to this day among the <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">Assyrian</a> Christians.
</p><p>Between 150 BC and 226 AD, Assyria changed hands between the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthian Empire</a> and the Romans until coming under the rule of the <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sasanian Empire</a> from 226–651, where it was known as <a href="/wiki/As%C5%8Drist%C4%81n" class="mw-redirect" title="Asōristān">Asōristān</a>.
</p><p>A number of at least partly neo-Assyrian kingdoms existed in the area between in the late classical and early Christian period also; <a href="/wiki/Adiabene" title="Adiabene">Adiabene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hatra" title="Hatra">Hatra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Osroene" title="Osroene">Osroene</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia_in_Classical_literature" title="Mesopotamia in Classical literature">Classical historiographers</a> and Biblical writers had only retained a fragmented, very dim and often inaccurate picture of Assyria. It was remembered that there had been an Assyrian empire predating the Persian one, but all particulars were lost. Thus Jerome's <i><a href="/wiki/Chronicon_(Jerome)" title="Chronicon (Jerome)">Chronicon</a></i> lists 36 kings of the Assyrians, beginning with <a href="/wiki/Ninus" title="Ninus">Ninus</a>, son of <a href="/wiki/Belus_(Assyrian)" title="Belus (Assyrian)">Belus</a>, down to <a href="/wiki/Sardanapalus" title="Sardanapalus">Sardanapalus</a>, the last king of the Assyrians before the empire fell to <a href="/wiki/Arbaces" title="Arbaces">Arbaces</a> the Median. Almost none of these have been substantiated as historical, with the exception of the Neo-Assyrian and Babylonian rulers listed in the <i><a href="/wiki/Canon_of_Kings" title="Canon of Kings">Canon of Kings</a></i>, beginning with <a href="/wiki/Nabonassar" title="Nabonassar">Nabonassar</a>.
</p><p>The Assyrians began to form and adopt a distinct <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Christianity" title="Eastern Christianity">Eastern Christianity</a>, with its accompanying <a href="/wiki/Syriac_literature" title="Syriac literature">Syriac literature</a>, between the 1st and 3rd centuries AD; however, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Mesopotamian_religion" title="Ancient Mesopotamian religion">ancient Mesopotamian religion</a> was still alive and well into the fourth century and pockets survived into the 10th century and possibly as late as the 17th century in <a href="/wiki/Mardin" title="Mardin">Mardin</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup><sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> However, the religion is now dead, and the Assyrian people, though still retaining Eastern Aramaic dialects as a mother tongue, are now wholly Christian.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Modern_discovery_of_Babylonia_and_Assyria" class="mw-redirect" title="Modern discovery of Babylonia and Assyria">modern discovery of Babylonia and Assyria</a> begins with excavations in <a href="/wiki/Nineveh" title="Nineveh">Nineveh</a> in 1845, which revealed the <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Ashurbanipal" title="Library of Ashurbanipal">Library of Ashurbanipal</a>. Decipherment of the <a href="/wiki/Cuneiform_script" class="mw-redirect" title="Cuneiform script">cuneiform script</a> was a formidable task that took more than a decade; but, by 1857, the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Asiatic_Society_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="Royal Asiatic Society of Great Britain and Ireland">Royal Asiatic Society of Great Britain and Ireland</a> was convinced that reliable reading of cuneiform texts was possible. <a href="/wiki/Assyriology" title="Assyriology">Assyriology</a> has since pieced together the formerly largely forgotten history of Mesopotamia. In the wake of the archaeological and philological rediscovery of ancient Assyria, <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_nationalism" title="Assyrian nationalism">Assyrian nationalism</a> became increasingly popular among the surviving remnants of the <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">Assyrian people</a>, who have come to strongly identify with ancient Assyria.
</p>

<li id="cite_note-Ragozin-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Ragozin_2-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Ragozin_2-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.çom/books?id=UPJ4DwAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PT5&amp;dq=Ragozin+%22Asshur+covered%22&amp;hl=en&amp;sa=X&amp;ved=0ahUKEwiZqY_H4JzfAhUTTBUIHfOhB1kQ6AEIKDAA#v=onepage&amp;q=Ragozin%20%22Asshur%20covered%22&amp;f=false">Zenaide Ragozin, <i>The Rise and Fall of the Assyrian Empire</i> (Ozymandias Press 2018), chapter 1, section 3</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template"><span style="white-space: nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Link_rot" title="Wikipedia:Link rot"><span title="&#160;Dead link since October 2019">permanent dead link</span></a></i>&#93;</span></sup>: "Aturia or Assyria proper" was a "small district of a few square miles". This gives a length of 350 miles by a breadth, between the Euphrates and the Zagros, varying from above 300 to 170 miles. 'The area was probably not less than 75,000 square miles'."</span>

<li id="cite_note-Parpola,_Simo_2004-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Parpola,_Simo_2004_15-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Compare: <cite class="citation journal">Parpola, Simo (2004). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://jaas.org/edocs/v18n2/Parpola-identity_Article%20-Final.pdf">"National and Ethnic Identity in the Neo-Assyrian Empire and Assyrian Identity in Post-Empire Times"</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>. <i>Journal of Assyrian Academic Studies</i>. <b>18</b> (2). <q>Disunited, dispersed in exile, and as dwindling minorities without full civil rights in their homelands, the Assyrians of today are in grave danger of total assimilation and extinction.[...] In order to survive as a nation, they must now unite under the Assyrian identity of their ancestors. It is the only identity that can help them to transcend the differences between them, speak with one voice again, catch the attention of the world, and regain their place among the nations.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Journal+of+Assyrian+Academic+Studies&amp;rft.atitle=National+and+Ethnic+Identity+in+the+Neo-Assyrian+Empire+and+Assyrian+Identity+in+Post-Empire+Times&amp;rft.volume=18&amp;rft.issue=2&amp;rft.date=2004&amp;rft.aulast=Parpola&amp;rft.aufirst=Simo&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fjaas.org%2Fedocs%2Fv18n2%2FParpola-identity_Article%2520-Final.pdf&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAssyria" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-H.W.F._Saggs-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-H.W.F._Saggs_18-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Saggs notes that: "the destruction of the Assyrian empire did not wipe out its population. They were predominantly peasant farmers and, since Assyria contains some of the best wheat land in the Near East, their descendants would, as opportunity permitted, build new villages over the old cities and carry on with agricultural life, remembering traditions of the former cities. After seven or eight centuries and various vicissitudes, these people became Christians" (<a href="#CITEREFSaggs1984">Saggs 1984</a>, p.&#160;290).</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-81"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-81">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://biblicalstudies.org.uk/article_attitude_wenham.html">The Old Testament Attitude to Homosexuality</a> by Gordon J Wenham, <i>Expository Times</i> 102.9 (1991): 259–363.</span>

<li id="cite_note-AK2013-82"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-AK2013_82-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite id="CITEREFKillebrew2013" class="citation">Killebrew, Ann E. (2013), <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=gBCl2IQfNioC&amp;pg=PA1#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false">"The Philistines and Other "Sea Peoples" in Text and Archaeology"</a>, <i>Society of Biblical Literature Archaeology and biblical studies</i>, Society of Biblical Lit, <b>15</b>, p.&#160;2, <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-58983-721-8" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-58983-721-8"><bdi>978-1-58983-721-8</bdi></a></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Society+of+Biblical+Literature+Archaeology+and+biblical+studies&amp;rft.atitle=The+Philistines+and+Other+%22Sea+Peoples%22+in+Text+and+Archaeology&amp;rft.volume=15&amp;rft.pages=2&amp;rft.date=2013&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-58983-721-8&amp;rft.aulast=Killebrew&amp;rft.aufirst=Ann+E.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DgBCl2IQfNioC%26pg%3DPA1%23v%3Donepage%26q%26f%3Dfalse&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAssyria" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/> Quote: "First coined in 1881 by the French Egyptologist G. Maspero (1896), the somewhat misleading term "Sea Peoples" encompasses the ethnonyms Lukka, Sherden, Shekelesh, Teresh, Eqwesh, Denyen, Sikil / Tjekker, Weshesh, and Peleset (Philistines). [Footnote: The modern term "Sea Peoples" refers to peoples that appear in several New Kingdom Egyptian texts as originating from "islands" (tables 1–2; Adams and Cohen, this volume; see, e.g., <a href="/wiki/Robert_Drews" title="Robert Drews">Drews</a> 1993, 57 for a summary). The use of quotation marks in association with the term "Sea Peoples" in our title is intended to draw attention to the problematic nature of this commonly used term. It is noteworthy that the designation "of the sea" appears only in relation to the Sherden, Shekelesh, and Eqwesh. Subsequently, this term was applied somewhat indiscriminately to several additional ethnonyms, including the Philistines, who are portrayed in their earliest appearance as invaders from the north during the reigns of Merenptah and Ramesses Ill (see, e.g., Sandars 1978; Redford 1992, 243, n. 14; for a recent review of the primary and secondary literature, see Woudhuizen 2006). Hencefore the term Sea Peoples will appear without quotation marks.]"</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Drews48-83"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Drews48_83-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Robert Drews, <i><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=bFpK6aXEWN8C&amp;pg=PA48&amp;lpg=PA48">The End of the Bronze Age: Changes in Warfare and the Catastrophe Ca. 1200 B.C.</a></i> pp. 48–61. Quote: "The thesis that a great "migration of the Sea Peoples" occurred ca. 1200 B.C. is supposedly based on Egyptian inscriptions, one from the reign of Merneptah and another from the reign of Ramesses III. Yet in the inscriptions themselves such a migration nowhere appears. After reviewing what the Egyptian texts have to say about 'the sea peoples', one Egyptologist (Wolfgang Helck) recently remarked that although some things are unclear, "eins ist aber sicher: Nach den agyptischen Texten haben wir es nicht mit einer 'Volkerwanderung' zu tun." Thus the migration hypothesis is based not on the inscriptions themselves but on their interpretation."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-84"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-84">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.fr/books?id=nhsmDwAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PA192&amp;lpg=PA192&amp;dq=a+companion+to+Assyria+assur-uballit+failed+attempt+to+reconquer+Harran&amp;source=bl&amp;ots=gABFtWPDrs&amp;sig=CE4sWHfyLy1HbBT9-chMz5AEg48&amp;hl=fr&amp;sa=X&amp;ved=0ahUKEwjs-bX9s_jaAhVEvBQKHXJ0CIoQ6AEIMTAA#v=onepage&amp;q=a%20companion%20to%20Assyria%20assur-uballit%20failed%20attempt%20to%20reconquer%20Harran&amp;f=false/">A Companion to Assyria</a>: p 192</span>
